REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This patient is a 46-year-old female brought in for first episode of seizure described as shaking all over, tongue biting, history of left-sided AVM with clips.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin and Lovenox.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded during lethargy.  Activating procedures were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  Activities during the procedure were not performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen almost continuously and frequently at T5 with a large field that spread over the left hemisphere. 
Focal slowing seen continuously over the left hemisphere maximal at T5 and frequency at 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.
SEIZURES:  The posterior temporal sharp waves at T5 appear to start and stop but at times appear to evolve in frequency and increase in amplitude and then suddenly stop and start again.  There appears to be no clinical correlation to this burst that may last for minutes but are most consistent with electrographic seizures.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to recurring electrographic left posterior temporal seizures, in addition to left posterior temporal focal cerebral dysfunction, which is consistent with the known AVM.  In addition, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific with regards to activity and may be due at least in part due to medication affect.  These findings were reported to the primary team as soon as the EEG was read at 7:00 p.m. on 10/22/2012.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 46-year-old female with left posterior temporal AVM and clips brought in with first episode of seizure described as shaking all over with tongue biting.
MEDICATIONS:  Chlorhexidine and Dilantin.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead  EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while confused, unresponsive, and lethargic.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 5-7 Hz and there is some frontal central beta.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  Activating procedures were not performed. 
Abnormal discharges:
Frequent T5 sharp waves seen throughout the recording.  At times the sharp waves would suddenly become rhythmic and continue for about 2-3 minutes and then stop.  They did not clearly evolve, but became extremely arrhythmic throughout this time.  
Focal slowing seen at T5 at a frequency of 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-60 mV.  
Moderate diffuse slowing at 5-7 Hz seen continuously throughout the recording. 
Seizures:  As above mentioned there is bursts of T5 sharp waves that did not clearly evolve, but would start and stop suddenly, at times for minutes with no clinical correlate.  These are concerning for possible electrographic seizures.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left posterior temporal sharp waves and monomorphic discharges concerning for electrographic seizures, in addition to generalized slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with seizures likely arising from the left posterior temporal region.  Note:  The bursts of rhythmic sharp waves were concerning for electrographic seizures, as they had no clinical correlate.  In addition, there is evidence of moderate diffuse slowing, which may due at least in part to medication effect.  These findings were reported to the primary team who decided to treat the patient and will place the patient on long term monitoring.


REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This patient is a 46-year-old female brought in for first episode of seizure described as shaking all over, tongue biting, history of left-sided AVM with clips.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 46-year-old woman with first episode of seizures and a known left occipital AVM.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin and Keppra.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with addition of anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during lethargy and confusion.  No activating procedures were performed.   Continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software and nurses' notes as well as patient alarms were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  During the last 8 hours of the recording, there was multiple electrode artifact as they were detached.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record remains without background which has a very seldom posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 7-8 Hz.  There are no activating procedures and there is some frontal central beta seen.  Overall, however, is mainly diffusely slow and a theta 5-7 Hz frequency.  Some sleep architecture can be seen.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen continuously at T5 at a frequency of 1-2 Hz in a monomorphic fashion consistent with periodic lateralizing epileptiform discharges (PLEDs).  
Focal slow waves are seen over the left hemisphere, maximal at T5 continuously throughout the recording at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.
Generalized slow waves at 5-7 Hz is seen throughout the recording continuously.
SEIZURES:  The patient had multiple electrographic seizures that would appear to arise from the left hemisphere, maximally over the posterior temporal region at T5 intervals and a delta rhythmic discharge that increases in frequency and amplitude and becomes better formed until it suddenly stops.  This first seizure occurred at 10:43:18 a.m. and ended at 10:45 a.m. A second similar seizure occurred at 4:21:57 p.m. and ended at 4:25:51 p.m. and the last seizure occurred around 10:50 p.m.  At that point, multiple electrodes had been lost which made it difficult to evaluate the origin of the seizure but appeared to be similar to the other ones.  Please note that during none of these seizures there appeared to be a clinical correlate.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal electroencephalogram due to left hemispheric seizures arising possibly from the left posterior temporal region in addition to left posterior temporal periodic lateralizing epileptiform discharges and focal slow waves in addition to generalized slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with multiple seizures arising from the left hemisphere, likely posterior temporal region.  In addition, there is evidence of acute structural  in the left posterior temporal region.  Lastly, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction which is nonspecific in regards to etiology.  These findings were continuously reported to the primary team to make adjustments in treatment based on the seizures.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 46-year-old woman, admitted with increasing seizure frequency.  Her seizures are felt to be secondary to AVM.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin and Keppra.
CONDITION OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with an additional T1 and T2 electrode and a single EKG lead.  Seizure detection software was activated.  Nursing notes and medical staff notes were reviewed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is significant right to left interhemispheric asymmetry, with slower waveforms over the left hemisphere.  During maximal wakefulness, the background over the right hemisphere consists of a mixture of polymorphic low to moderate amplitude theta and delta without expected posterior dominant rhythm or anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background over the left hemisphere consists of a mixture of polymorphic low to moderate amplitude theta and delta activity with additional polymorphic delta over the right temporal region.  No posterior dominant rhythm or anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient is observed over the left hemisphere.  During sleep, sleep spindle-like complexes are captured.  Continuous moderate amplitude sharp waves with after-going slow waves are captured over the left posterior temporal region.  Intermittently throughout the recording, these discharges occur rhythmically at approximately 1 to 2 Hz and correspond to periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges.  Approximately 8 electrographic seizures are captured.  They start with increasing frequency epileptiform discharges, and rapidly evolve into rhythmic theta and later alpha, most prominent over the left posterior quadrant.  As seizure progresses, these discharges spread throughout the left hemisphere and decrease in frequency to about 3.5 Hz delta range.  These seizures last approximately 3 to 4 minutes.  There is no clinical correlate that is evident from the video review.  From approximately 4 p.m. until approximately 5:30 p.m., several leads are intermittently disconnected.  This limits interpretation of the EEG during that time period.
FINDINGS:  
Focal left hemispheric slowing.
Frequent left posterior quadrant epileptiform discharges that intermittently organize into 1 to 2 Hz PLEDS.  
Approximately 8 electrographic seizures that start over the left posterior quadrant and spread throughout the left hemisphere.  These seizures do not have a clear clinical correlate.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 24-hour continuous audio-video EEG due to:
Moderate diffuse slowing and disorganization.
Focal left hemispheric slowing.
Frequent left posterior quadrant epileptiform discharges that are frequently periodic.
Approximately 8 electrographic seizures lasting 3 to 4 minutes with onset over the left posterior quadrant.   
Diffuse slowing indicates nonspecific cerebral dysfunction that in this case could partially be related to the medication effect.  Focal left hemispheric slowing and epileptiform discharges indicate structural pathology over that region that has high epileptogenic potential.  There is an epileptogenic focus over the left posterior quadrant.  When compared to the EEG from the prior 24 hours, there has been no significant change in the degree of the background slowing or frequency of seizures or epileptiform discharges.  These findings were conveyed to the primary team.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is an adult with a history of closed head injury late 2012, significant bifrontal encephalomalacia, recently discharged from rehab but found to have a right temporal subdural hematoma.MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Keppra and Ativan.INTRODUCTION:  Continuous video EEG monitoring was performed in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG.  The patient was somnolent but intermittently agitated.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: February 11- 12 is markedly abnormal.  Throughout much of the record, it is primarily a slow theta pattern on the right.  The left hemisphere demonstrates epileptiform activity with complex field of spread typically most remarkable in the left posterotemporal or parietal region but sometimes in the left frontal region as well. Intermittent bursts in this periodic complex epileptiform activity were noted, sometimes with associated theta frequency activity and on 2 occasions at 9:30 and at 10:20 p.m. with a bit more sustained bursts.   The patient also had intermittent arousals although he was not particularly interactive.  After receiving 4 mg of Ativan for both of these, the overall background was significantly slower and the EEG transitioned more to a periodic complex in the left hemisphere with polyphasic activity and fast activity.  The overall background remained quite slow with intermittent bilateral attenuation.  Stimulation of the patient would produce an alerting response.   This first 24 hour section starts in the afternoon and by the morning of the 12th, there is an increase in faster frequency activity.  Sustained bursts are noted with some frequency evolution including at 9:40 a.m. but stimulation of the patient also produces an increase in faster frequency activity. By 9:53 a.m. the patient is somewhat interactive with the nurse and intermittently attempts to get out of bed.  When he does so, there is a bit more faster frequency activity on the right but the epileptiform activity on the left really remains unchanged with a periodic pattern with some buildup but without definitive frequency evolution. Additional doses of Ativan were given on the morning of the 12th, again producing attenuation on the right and faster frequency activity on the left.   Heart rate 96 BPM. Events at 11:26 p.m. and 11:51 p.m. on the evening of the 11th were not associated with definitive epileptiform discharges and may have represented arousals.   FEBRUARY 12 THROUGH 13INTRODUCTION:  During this 24-hour section of the EEG, the patient received Ativan, Keppra, Dilantin and Serax 60 mg three times daily.  The patient remained relatively obtunded, intermittently arousable.DESCRIPTION OF RECORD:  The background EEG remains markedly abnormal with a relatively low voltage and slow pattern on the right.  The left hemisphere demonstrates a periodic complex with a relatively large field of spread, maximum at P3 T5, sometimes involving the left anterior temporal region.  It waxes and wanes, does not disappear and there is some buildup noted intermittently.   There are bursts of epileptiform activity which show some frequency evolution but without the characteristic start and stop of a typical epileptiform seizure, however particularly at 2:50 a.m. on the morning of the 13th, the pattern described above is certainly one of a late status epilepticus pattern.   By 8:00 a.m., the epileptiform activity seems to be waning a bit but remains continuous.  Only the periodic nature of it is slowing a bit.   A more clearly defined seizure is noted with more clear frequency evolution at 9:52.  Seizure onset is difficult to determine but there is clearly an increase in epileptiform activity.  No clinical signs are noted and this discharge seems to be stopping.  The seizure is about 3 minutes in duration but seizure on and offset is difficult to define.  Intermittent bursts of sharp waves and spikes are noted out of the left hemisphere in an otherwise bilaterally suppressed background without so much buildup. Stimulation of the patient at 2 p.m. produces an increase in faster frequency activity in the background. Additional subclinical seizures are noted at 2:21.  The patient is responsive during the seizures with eye opening and looking about despite the epileptiform pattern. Heart rate 96 BPM.IMPRESSION:  When reviewed at a slower paper speed, this EEG monitoring session is really most compatible with a late status epilepticus pattern.  Subclinical seizures were noted in an individual who is confused but actually responsive during the seizures.CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is an adult with a history of closed head injury late 2012, significant bifrontal encephalomalacia, recently discharged from rehab but found to have a right temporal subdural hematoma.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Keppra and Ativan.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous video EEG monitoring was performed in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG.  The patient was somnolent but intermittently agitated.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: 
February 11- 12 is markedly abnormal.  Throughout much of the record, it is primarily a slow theta pattern on the right.  The left hemisphere demonstrates epileptiform activity with complex field of spread typically most remarkable in the left posterotemporal or parietal region but sometimes in the left frontal region as well. 
Intermittent bursts in this periodic complex epileptiform activity were noted, sometimes with associated theta frequency activity and on 2 occasions at 9:30 and at 10:20 p.m. with a bit more sustained bursts.   
The patient also had intermittent arousals although he was not particularly interactive.  After receiving 4 mg of Ativan for both of these, the overall background was significantly slower and the EEG transitioned more to a periodic complex in the left hemisphere with polyphasic activity and fast activity.  The overall background remained quite slow with intermittent bilateral attenuation.  Stimulation of the patient would produce an alerting response.   
This first 24 hour section starts in the afternoon and by the morning of the 12th, there is an increase in faster frequency activity.  Sustained bursts are noted with some frequency evolution including at 9:40 a.m. but stimulation of the patient also produces an increase in faster frequency activity. 
By 9:53 a.m. the patient is somewhat interactive with the nurse and intermittently attempts to get out of bed.  When he does so, there is a bit more faster frequency activity on the right but the epileptiform activity on the left really remains unchanged with a periodic pattern with some buildup but without definitive frequency evolution. 
Additional doses of Ativan were given on the morning of the 12th, again producing attenuation on the right and faster frequency activity on the left.   
Heart rate 96 BPM. 
Events at 11:26 p.m. and 11:51 p.m. on the evening of the 11th were not associated with definitive epileptiform discharges and may have represented arousals.   
INTRODUCTION:  During this 24-hour section of the EEG, the patient received Ativan, Keppra, Dilantin and Serax 60 mg three times daily.  The patient remained relatively obtunded, intermittently arousable.
DESCRIPTION OF RECORD:  The background EEG remains markedly abnormal with a relatively low voltage and slow pattern on the right.  The left hemisphere demonstrates a periodic complex with a relatively large field of spread, maximum at P3 T5, sometimes involving the left anterior temporal region.  It waxes and wanes, does not disappear and there is some buildup noted intermittently.   
There are bursts of epileptiform activity which show some frequency evolution but without the characteristic start and stop of a typical epileptiform seizure, however particularly at 2:50 a.m. on the morning of the 13th, the pattern described above is certainly one of a late status epilepticus pattern.   
By 8:00 a.m., the epileptiform activity seems to be waning a bit but remains continuous.  Only the periodic nature of it is slowing a bit.   
A more clearly defined seizure is noted with more clear frequency evolution at 9:52.  Seizure onset is difficult to determine but there is clearly an increase in epileptiform activity.  No clinical signs are noted and this discharge seems to be stopping.  The seizure is about 3 minutes in duration but seizure on and offset is difficult to define.  Intermittent bursts of sharp waves and spikes are noted out of the left hemisphere in an otherwise bilaterally suppressed background without so much buildup. 
Stimulation of the patient at 2 p.m. produces an increase in faster frequency activity in the background. 
Additional subclinical seizures are noted at 2:21.  The patient is responsive during the seizures with eye opening and looking about despite the epileptiform pattern. 
Heart rate 96 BPM.
IMPRESSION:  When reviewed at a slower paper speed, this EEG monitoring session is really most compatible with a late status epilepticus pattern.  Subclinical seizures were noted in an individual who is confused but actually responsive during the seizures.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is an adult with a history of closed head injury late 2012, significant bifrontal encephalomalacia, recently discharged from rehab but found to have a right temporal subdural hematoma.

INTRODUCTION:  During this 24-hour section of the EEG, the patient received Ativan, Keppra, Dilantin and Serax 60 mg three times daily.  The patient remained relatively obtunded, intermittently arousable.
DESCRIPTION OF RECORD:  The background EEG remains markedly abnormal with a relatively low voltage and slow pattern on the right. By 8:00 a.m., the epileptiform activity seems to be waning a bit but remains continuous.  Only the periodic nature of it is slowing a bit.   
A more clearly defined seizure is noted with more clear frequency evolution at 9:52.  Seizure onset is difficult to determine but there is clearly an increase in epileptiform activity. Heart rate 96 BPM.
IMPRESSION:  When reviewed at a slower paper speed, this EEG monitoring session is really most compatible with a late status epilepticus pattern.  Subclinical seizures were noted in an individual who is confused but actually responsive during the seizures.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  58-year-old man with bifrontal bur holes and evacuation of subdural hematomas with change in mental status, with the wife noting that he is lip smacking and staring but never returned to baseline.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Keppra, Vimpat.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  Patient was recorded while lethargic.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software as well as patient notes and nurse's notes and patient alarms were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  Overall, the recording appears to be mainly in the delta frequencies with some intermittent faster frequencies showing up periodically.  There are some stage changes noted.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen continuously at T5/P3 with a periodicity that ranges from 0.5 Hz to 2 Hz, at times becoming slightly less organized.  These are consistent with periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLEDs).
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-40 mV.
SEIZURES:  Although there were about 2-3 rhythmic runs of the PLEDs, there were 2 electrographic seizures.  One occurred at 4:15 p.m. lasting for a minute and a half.  The 2nd one occurred at 9:43 p.m.  Both of them did not appear to have any clear clinical correlate.  They were comprised of rhythmic P3-4/T5 sharp waves, evolving in frequency and amplitude and then suddenly stopping.
HEART RATE:  84.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left posterior temporal and parietal periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLEDs) and electrographic seizures in addition to generalized slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals left posterior temporal and parietal seizures as well as a focus of irritability and cerebral dysfunction in that region.  This is likely related to the known lesion in that region.  In addition, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral  dysfunction, which is nonspecific with regards to etiology and may be due at least in part to medication effect.
	


CLINICAL HISTORY:  33 year old female with history of severe mental retardation, cerebral palsy, and seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Dilantin, Ativan.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long term monitoring  EEG was performed at bedside  using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness.  No activating procedures were performed.  Continuous spike and wave detection software was performed for seizure detection, as well as patient alarm for detection of events.  Also the nurses reported events.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm nor frontal central beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture and no activating procedures were performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
As seen before, focal sharp waves in multiple places, including F4, T3, P3, and T5, as well as frontal polyspikes seen throughout the recording.  
Generalized slow waves 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-70 mV were seen throughout the recording. 
SEIZURES:  Throughout the recording there is about 15 seizures seen throughout the recording where the patient suddenly lifts her arms tonically and her face also opens in a tonic fashion.  This episode lasts 15-20 seconds and then stops.  There appears to be a sudden attenuation diffusely throughout the recording.  There seems to be a paroxysm of fast that cannot be localized to one specific region, followed by tonic muscle artifact throughout the recording and diffuse slowing at the end of the seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Multiple tonic seizures of generalized onset. 
Multifocal spikes.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of generalized epilepsy with multiple seizures of unclear onset, as well as multiple focus of possible seizures of brain irritability and seizure onset.  In addition, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regards to etiology and may be due to the patient's overall cognitive deficits or disease. These findings were reported to the primary team.


REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 33-year-old female, blind with cerebral palsy, severe mental retardation, and seizure disorder with recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, phenobarbital, and Keppra.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed for 48 hours, using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software along with patient alarms were used for reviewing the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm and/or frontocentral beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed. 
Abnormal discharges:  
Focal sharp waves were seen in multiple places including F8/T2, F7/T3, T5, C4, and P3.  In addition to this, there are bursts of polyspike and wave at 2 Hz seen intermittently throughout the recording on both days.  There is no clinical correlation to these polyspike and wave discharges.
At times there were, during the first 24 hours more frequently than the last 24, discharges of fast beta frequency discharges with an abrupt ending seen at T5/P3 and at times at P4/T6 with no clear correlate.  They would last for about 10 to 15 seconds and then stop. 
Seizures:  The patient would develop sudden onset of bilateral arm raising and mouth opening for about 10 to 15 seconds and then stop.  During this, the EEG would suddenly attenuate and there would be a fast beta pattern seen diffusely followed by muscle artifact.  During this recording, the patient had only 3 of these in the past 2 days.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to multifocal spikes, diffuse slowing, some electrographic seizures, and some clinical seizures.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific with regard to etiology.  In addition, there is evidence of multiple focal irritability and diffuse seizures consistent with generalized symptomatic epilepsy.  Please note that this recording has markedly improved since the beginning and the clinical seizures have almost subsided and only 3 were seen in the past 48 hours.  These findings were reported to the primary team.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  33 year old female with a history of severe MR, cerebral palsy, and seizures, with multiple seizures today.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Dilantin, Ativan.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Recurrent seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded while awake and asleep.  The record was reviewed using continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software and patient noted events, as well as review of the tracings.

ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:

Generalized slow waves at 2 to 4 Hz with an amplitude of 20 to 70 microvolts seen throughout the recording.

These last for about 15 to 20 seconds and then stop.  In the EEG there appears to be a sudden attenuation seen diffusely throughout the recording consistent with paroxysmal fasts that cannot be localized to one specific region followed by tonic muscle artifact seen throughout the recording and followed by diffuse slowing at the end of the seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:  
multiple tonic seizures of generalized unclear onset. 
multifocal spikes.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of symptomatic generalized epilepsy with multiple seizures of unclear onset as well as multiple focus for origination of seizures.  In addition, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction which is nonspecific with regard to etiology and may be due to the patient's overall cognitive disease.

Generalized seizures.  
Polyspike discharges.

CLINICAL HISTORY:   43  year old woman with epilepsy since age 32 with 4 seizures per month.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Topamax.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.  The patient reported her last seizure more than a week prior to admission, but was somewhat distressed upon admission and difficult to console.  Continuous seizure and spike detection software was utilized.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  April 23 to April 24, 2012:   Wakefulness demonstrates a well organized background.  There is some problems overnight with FP1 electrode.  On rare occasions there is some subtle left temporal theta.  This is seen in wakefulness and on rare occasions in sleep.  Using an average reference there is some discreet, rare left temporal sharp waves at F7 in sleep.  The theta described on the left is quite infrequent and the vast majority of the record demonstrates normal wakefulness through stage II sleep. 
April 24 through April 25, 2012:  During this section of the record the patient experienced no clinical seizures.  This section of the record is characterized by a symmetric reactive 9 Hz alpha rhythm.  Drowsiness includes rhythmic background slowing with vertex waves, K-complexes, and spindles.  The patient is asleep in the afternoon and awake a fair amount during the day.  Unfortunately there is a fair amount of electrode artifact in many sections of the record.  There is a paucity of focal or 
epileptiform activity and in fact in comparison to previous EEGs for this patient, the overall EEG is well organized.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG was utilized with seizure and spike detection.  Unfortunately, the patient’s prosthetic hair was a serious impediment interfering with the electrodes.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The patient had a seizure at 12:34 on April 26, which was completely unlocalized by the EEG, in part due to artifact and then subsequently by the patient’s removal of the EEG electrodes during the seizures. 
This seizure and its postictal changes were at least 4 minutes in duration.  The patient was eating.  She stopped eating, she was looking away from the camera.  She had automatisms with her left foot and then she looked away from the camera.  For many minutes, either during or after the seizure, she had automatisms of the hands and face with hand washing movements.  She was able to push the tray back from the bed.  She seemed like she was about to fall of the bed, but actually was able to get herself back on the bed. Random wakefulness and sleep in the hours following, including in sleep, there were right temporal sharp waves noted.  Vertex waves and spindles were noted in stage II sleep. 
In the additional recording on the morning of April 27, there were a handful of sharp waves on the left, but more significant sharp waves on the right. The technologist continued to work with the patient, including wrapping the scalp, but with limited success in terms of maintaining the quality of the EEG recording.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous video EEG monitoring was utilized using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with seizure and spike detection.  The patient had no seizures.  None were identified by the computerized seizure and spike detector.  She has braids and it is a challenge to keep her electrodes in place.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  In wakefulness, there is a 9 Hz alpha rhythm.  Stage II sleep includes vertex phase, K-complexes, and spindles. The automated seizure detectors identify only artifacts.   Particularly in the early morning hours, there is significant electrode problems.   
HR:    60 bpm  
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous video EEG monitoring is performed in the unit using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG.  No clinical seizures were reported.  The patient has extensive braids.  Her head was wrapped in an attempt to try and preserve the electrode integrity.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There are sections where the electrode integrity is pretty good.  Unfortunately, there are also other sections where it is not, particularly at C3.   
Although there are no clinical seizures noted, there is a prominent burst of rhythmic delta from the right temporal region with a sharp wave at 12:29.  This did not appear to have triggered the seizure and spike detector.  The patient is lying on her side.  She moves about afterward.  Although this is highly suspicious for a subclinical seizure, seizure onset and offset is difficult to tell and there are other electrode problems.  Sleep is characterized by vertex waves and spindles.  The patient spent most of the day in bed. Unfortunately, every time the patient moves around, it triggers the seizure and spike detector, which makes it very difficult to rely on the seizure and spike detector for epileptiform seizures.   
HR:    60 to 80 bpm
IMPRESSION:  This continuous video EEG monitoring recording was remarkable for:
Interictal epileptiform activity which included sharp waves, primarily seen on the right. 
One clear complex partial seizure which was not adequately localized by scalp and part due to technical difficulties.
Question subclinical seizure at midday on the 27th, again with some technical issues due to electrode issues.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is diagnostic of a focal mechanism for epilepsy based on the interictal spikes and the seizure semiology on the video, but the data obtained is not adequate for a presurgical localization.

MEDICATIONS:  Tegretol, Ativan

DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  As the tracing begins, the patient is postictal and the EEG is diffusely slow with a lower voltage delta pattern and bursts of theta.   
Out of the slow and suppressed pattern, there is an increase in amplitude with rhythmic 2-3 Hz activity from the right temporal region maximum at T2 at 4:42:05.  The clinical features of the seizure include motion arrest.  Then the patient has eye flutter which is more prominent on the right.  He has eye blinking and oral automatisms.  He is initially responsive during the beginning of the seizure.  He has oral automatisms, eye flutter followed by postictal obtundation.

When the seizure is over, the background EEG is markedly slow and the patient is quite somnolent.  He rolls over on his stomach and it becomes very difficult to maintain good quality electrodes.  He is markedly obtunded postictally. 
HR:    96 bpm

An electrographic seizure which was clinically a complex partial seizure out of the right temporal region.
Generalized background slowing.

DATE OF STUDY:  11/19/2012 to 11/20/2012, for a total of 17 hours.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 67-year-old woman found covered in urine and feces with a glucose of 1200 and with myoclonus, has a previous possible left MCA infarct and brain atrophy on the CT scan.
MEDICATIONS:  Ativan and fosphenytoin.
INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed from 11/19/2012 to 11/20/2012 using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with the addition of anterior temple single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recording will unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed.  Overall the background is consistent of diffuse delta slowing at 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.  There is also some burst suppression seen intermittently.  Superimposed over this background are rhythmic low amplitude of what appears to be polyspikes which at times appear to have a left frontal predominance and sometimes right frontal predominance, and at times they are seen diffusely followed by a beta discharge.  During everyone of these discharges the patient has a jerk which moves her head towards the left and at times towards the right.  In addition to this continuous discharge that occurs at times there is an O2 more than O1 beta discharge that starts suddenly and appears to increase in frequency and then slow down and stop suddenly, occurring frequently throughout the recording.  There is no clinical correlate with this second discharge.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-30 microvolts with intermittent suppression seen for 2-3 seconds.  
Bursts of polyspikes of low amplitude and followed by beta discharges seen diffusely, at times with a left frontal and sometimes a right frontal predominance with associated jerking consistent with myoclonus.  
Multiple frequent electrographic seizures arising from the right occipital region consisting of beta evolving in frequency and amplitude which have a sudden onset and end.
IMPRESSION:  
Abnormal electroencephalography due to generalized polyspikes with clinical myoclonus as well as multiple right occipital electrographic seizures and marked diffuse slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with severe cerebral dysfunction and irritability with clinical myoclonus, which is consistent with a severe diffuse injury which could be seen with diffuse anoxic injury and sometimes with diffuse metabolic injury.  In addition, there is focal electrographic seizures arising from the right occipital region.  The seizures appear to continue throughout the recording.The myoclonus seems to improve during the last hours of the recording, but not the occipital seizures.  These findings were informed to the primary team as the EEG was reviewed.

Rhythmic data discharge in the occipital region that at the beginning of the recording appears to evolve, but later it stops evolving and becomes much shorter and with a definite start and stop. CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 65-year-old woman with a history of left parieto-occipital lung metastasis removed approximately 3 weeks ago.  She now presents with change in mental status that is accompanied by several convulsions.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Dilantin and Decadron.
INTRODUCTION:  Conditions of the Recording:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead.  This is a 24-hour continuous video EEG recording.  The recording was reviewed on several occasions throughout the 24-hour period.  Medical staff notes and nursing staff notes were reviewed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is right to left interhemispheric asymmetry with slower waveforms and high amplitudes over the left hemisphere.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the right hemisphere consists of low amplitude polymorphic 8-9 Hz activity.  There is anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient over the right hemisphere.  There is no clear posterior dominant rhythm over the left hemisphere and the anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient is weak.  There are continuous semirhythmic and rhythmic moderate amplitude sharp waves with upgoing slow waves observed over the left posterior quadrant, with maximal scalp negativity over T5 and 01 electrodes.  Frequently, these epileptiform discharges are accompanied by low amplitude fast activity.  Frequency of these discharges on average is approximately 1 Hz.  Additionally, they frequently transform into brief poorly organized runs of polymorphic theta and delta with imbedded sharp waves.  This EEG pattern represents ictal interictal continuum.  On several occasions, frank discrete electrographic seizures are  observed.  These start with approximately 1 Hz sharp waves with upgoing slow waves with accompanied fast activity observed over the left posterior quadrant.  This activity increases in frequency into the theta range.  It gradually evolves into approximately 2-1/2 to 3 Hz sharp wave contoured left posterior quadrant delta activity that spreads to the left temporal leads.  These seizures have poorly defined electrographic onset.  They last approximately 60 seconds and in example of such, a seizure occurs at about 6:03 p.m.  As the recording progresses, such seizures become better defined.  These seizures occur several times an hour.  Additionally, as the recording progresses, diffuse polymorphic theta and delta activity are captured and periods of discontinuity are noted.  No normal sleep architecture is captured, however, periods of quiescence are noted.  Starting at approximately 8 p.m., there is progressive electrode artifact that eventually affects multiple leads.  The leads were finally fixed at approximately 8:12 a.m.
FINDINGS:  
Ictal interictal continuum emanating from the left posterior quadrant early in the recording.
Discrete focal 15-90 second left posterior quadrant seizures, that are defined as the recording progresses and occur several times per hour in the second half of the recording.
Continuous approximately 1 Hz PLEDs, quasi-PLEDs and PLEDs plus in the left posterior quadrant, with maximal scalp negativity over T5 and 01 electrodes.
Focal left posterior quadrant slowing.
Increase in degree of slowing as the recording progresses.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 24-hour continuous video EEG monitoring due to left posterior quadrant continuous rhythmic and semi-rhythmic epileptiform discharges, left posterior quadrant ictal interictal continuum, and left posterior quadrant discrete electrographic seizures that become better defined as the recording progresses.  Additionally, there is left posterior quadrant focal slowing.  These findings indicate focal cerebral dysfunction over the left posterior quadrant, and in this patient consistent with a history of prior left parieto-occipital lobe resection.  These findings were conveyed to the primary team.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 65-year-old woman with recent left parietoccipital removal of lung metastasis, who now presents with change in mental status and several convulsions.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Dilantin, Depakote, phenobarbital, versed, and Decadron.
INTRODUCTION:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead.  The recording was performed continuously at the patient's bedside.  Video capability was activated.  Nursing staff notes and medical staff notes were reviewed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior-to-posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  There is isolated left anterior hemispheric asymmetry with slow wave forms and a high amplitude over the left hemisphere.  The background over the right hemisphere consists of a mixture of polymorphic low-to-moderate amplitude, mixture of theta and delta activity.  The background over the left hemisphere consists of a mixture of polymorphic theta and delta mixed with continuous approximately 1 Hz semi-rhythmic and rhythmic sharp waves with after-going slow waves with maximum scalp negativity over T5 and L1 electrodes.  As the recording progresses, amplitude periodicity and frequency of the discharges becomes less pronounced.  Diffuse periods of discontinuity as well as increasing degree of bursts of diffuse beta are captured.  Occasional left posterior quadrant electrographic seizures are captured.  Frequency of these seizures decreases as the recording progresses.  A left side electrographic seizure is calculated at approximately 2:16 p.m.  Seizure starts with approximately 1 Hz rhythmic sharp waves with after-going slow waves with rapidly decrease in amplitude and evolved into the sharply contoured theta activity with embedded 2 Hz sharp waves with after-going slow waves.  This activity evolves into approximately 2.5-3 Hz sharply contoured delta activity with under-riding faster frequencies and spreads throughout the left hemisphere.  That seizure was approximately 40 seconds.  No normal sleep architecture is observed.
FINDINGS:  
Occasional left posterior quadrant focal brief electrographic seizures that decrease in frequency as the recording progresses, with left __________ seizure at approximately 2:16 p.m.
Continuous left posterior quadrant rhythmic and semi-rhythmic epileptiform discharges (rather than quasi PLEDs) that decrease in frequency, amplitude, and as the recording progresses.
Moderate diffuse background slowing with period of discontinuity, degree of which fluctuates throughout the recording.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 20-hour continuous audio EEG monitoring due to occasional left posterior quadrant seizures and continuous left posterior quadrant semi-periodic and periodic epileptiform discharges that decrease in frequency and periodicity as the recording progresses.  These findings indicate underlying focal cerebral dysfunction, likely structural in etiology and is consistent with this patient's history of recent tumor resection.  Diffuse background slowing indicates etiology for nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction and is consistent with this patient with a history of medication use.  Diffuse excess beta could be secondary to medication effect such as benzodiazepines and barbiturates.  These findings were conveyed to the primary team.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  Adult male status post gunshot wound to chest and back.  At 4:00 a.m. today the patient had generalized tonic-clonic seizures requiring 7 mg of Ativan.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Fentanyl, Ativan.

DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The background EEG is markedly abnormal and includes multiple electrographic seizures which are clinically associated with eye blink.  Much of the EEG is, in fact, ictal with seizures seeming to wax and wane.  It is difficult at this point to determine when seizures begin and end, but the very first seizure for this patient seems to begin with a burst of rhythmic higher amplitude sharply contoured activity on the left.  Many of the seizures are characterized by high amplitude rhythmic theta-delta or spike-and-wave activity which is essentially bilateral.  Some seizures end with T6 spiking.  Others end with rhythmic sharply contoured activity on the left.  As the recording continues, the seizures begin with faster frequency activity alpha and beta out of the frontal regions and then a paroxysmal evolution.  Some of the seizures do have a clearly defined ending with a slow background and then the T6 periodic sharp waves.  There are sections of the record where there is a T6 sharp wave and a T1 burst of sharply contoured delta.  
As the recording continues, more of the epileptiform activity is in the right hemisphere and may be posterotemporal or anterior temporal. 
By 12:34, the nurse is providing Ativan.  As the record ends at 12:40, epileptiform activity is noted and has not resolved. 
HR:    120 bpm
IMPRESSION:  Markedly abnormal EEG due to:
Multiple electrographic seizures which seem to emanate from the frontal regions but at least in some locations either begin or end with epileptiform activity in the left frontal or even right posterotemporal region.
Interictal epileptiform activity from multiple different localizations including T1, T3, T4, and T6. 
Background slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is diagnostic of a complex partial status epilepticus.  Based on these findings, continuous video EEG monitoring was initiated.

CLINICAL HISTORY: 45 year old right handed male with past CNS toxoplasmosis, encephalopathy, recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS: Keppra, Lorazepam, Phenobarbital
 
INTRODUCTION: Continuous digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. The patient is noted to have intermittent seizures characterized by eye blinking, grimacing and turning to the right. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: As the tracing begins on the afternoon of 10/12/2010, recurrent electrographic seizures are noted which begin with a buzz of fast activity, which is primarily in the left posterior quadrant including T5, 02 and P3. The activity demonstrates more of a generalized buzz and then repetitive sharp waves in the left posterior quadrant including T4, 01 and P3. Typical seizures are 1 to 2 minutes in duration. As the recording begins, brief seizures less than 1 minute are noted at 5:40 p.m. 
The seizures begin to abate overnight and they are not as continuous in the overnight sections of the recording. 
Lacosamide is administered late in the day on 10/12/2010 and in the morning of 10/13/2010. Despite this, there are seizures at 3:40 in the afternoon on 10/13/2010. Although this review ends at 4:48, following the initiation of phenobarbital, the seizures do appear to be slowing. Additional seizures are noted at 3:44. The patient is somewhat drowsy following the administration of phenobarbital. As the recording continues, the eye blinking and clinical features of seizures remain with eye blinking and posturing of the patient's arms. 
HR: 72 bpm. 
LONG TERM MONITORING 10/14/2010 - 10-114 
INTRODUCTION: Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. Occasional clinical seizures are noted by the team. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: Electrographic seizures are identified including at 6:07 p.m. The seizures are identical to those seen in the initial section with a buzz of 20 Hz activity which begins in the left occipital region. Using an average reference, this is clearly maximum at T5 and 01. This activity builds up and a beta activity spreads throughout the posterior quadrant on the left and then more diffusely throughout the left hemisphere. It is also picked up in the right occipital region. This is followed by repetitive spike activity, which is maximum in the left occipital region but again has a diffuse representation with frequency evolution noted over the first 30 seconds. Today the seizures abate in the left temporal region but continue a little bit more in the left parietal region and then are followed by background slowing.
 
The seizure detector is a reliable index for this patient and seizures are observed at 6:07 p.m., 7:36, 7:52, 8:05, 8:14, 8:22, 8:30, 8:34, 8:41, 8:49, 8:56, 9:03, 9:09, 9:34, 9:40 and 9:55. At this time, Ativan is given. With the last dose of Ativan, there is a dramatic decrease in the seizures after the 10 p.m. dose. There are seizure markings at 
5 a.m. which are not associated with seizures. 
Recording continues in the morning of 10/14/2010, and at this point, the patient is awake. The background is more of a theta frequency pattern. No additional seizures are noted. 
HR: 60 bpm 
IMPRESSION: CLINICAL CORRELATION: This 48 hour long term video EEG monitoring session is characterized by: Complex partial status epilepticus which improves dramatically in the first 24 hours of the record and in the second 24 hours, there are sections in the record with 1 seizure per hour to as many as 8 seizures per hour but with a dramatic decrease after the last dose of Ativan. At present, the patient's status epilepticus appears to have resolved.

Towards the end of this recording, there were left periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges noted in left posterior quadrant region lasting for several minutes with no evolution. Left periodic lateralized epileptiform CLINICAL CORRELATION:  Focal slowing is a consistent with functional or structural abnormality in left posterior quadrant. Clinical correlation and long-term monitoring are recommended. DATE OF THE RECORDING:  05/16/2013 from approximately 2:00 p.m. until 05/17/2013 until approximately 2:00 p.m.  Total time of the recording is approximately 24 hours.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 19-year-old woman with a history of seizures who now presents with recurrent complex partial seizures and a change in behavior, subsequently found to have status epilepticus.
CURRENT MEDICATIONS:  Lamictal, Versed, phenobarbital.
CONDITION OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.   
The recording was reviewed on several occasions during the 24 hour recording.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORDING:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency gradient.  The background consists mostly of a mixture of low amplitude delta activity with underwriting faster frequencies and 1 to 4 second diffuse bursts of spindle-like waveforms.  No clear normal architecture is captured.  Multiple electrographic seizures of variable duration are noted throughout the recording.  Seizure duration varies from 30 seconds to over 30 minutes.  Seizure onset is often poorly localized.  Some start with bilateral central frontal fast activity that increases in amplitude and decreases in frequency to the alpha range.  More prolonged seizures start with diffuse frontally predominant delta activity with underwriting by frontocentral faster activity.  This activity increases in amplitude and decreases in frequency, and spreads throughout other cerebral regions.  During multiple long seizures the seizure output is not clear.  There has been some tachycardia noted on a electrocardiogram lead.  During seizures heart rate increases to approximately 120 beats per minute.
FINDINGS:  
Intermittent electrographic seizures with poor localization and lateralization with variable durations from 30 seconds to approximately 30 minutes.  
Moderate to severe diffuse slowing and low amplitude attenuation.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 24 hour EEG due to intermittent seizures and frank status epilepticus as well as moderate to severe diffuse slowing.  The slowing indicates etiological nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction, and in this case likely related to medication effect.  These findings were conveyed to the Primary Team.

MEDICATIONS:  Current medications include:  Lamictal and 8 mg of Ativan.
INTRODUCTION:  Condition of the recording:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead. Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background consists of diffuse rhythmic and semi-rhythmic high amplitude frontally predominant delta activity mixed with bursts of diffuse spindle-like wave forms.  No normal sleep architecture is captured.  At least 3 electrographic seizures are noted. These seizures start with approximately a 10-12 second run of fast activity most pronounced over the right occipital lead.  This activity eventually spread to the right anterior region. During this spread, rhythmic more frequency theta and delta activity are captured over the right anterior leads with some reflection over the left hemisphere.  Seizure offset is gradual.  Total seizure duration is approximately one minute.  Increase in heart rate up to 150 to 180 beats a minutes is noted during seizure.  During one of the seizures, the patient bites on the ET tube.   
A single EKG lead demonstrates continued tachycardia with heart rate on average of approximately 120 beats a minute.
FINDINGS:

Runs of bilateral posterior fast activity. 
Moderate to severe diffuse slowing.
IMPRESSION:  This is an abnormal electroencephalogram due to moderate diffuse slowing, intermittent high frequency activity observed posteriorly, as well as 3 focal right hemisphere electrographic seizures with onset of right occipital leads.  Diffuse slowing indicates etiologically nonspecific cerebral dysfunction, that in this case could partially be related to medications.  There is a seizure onset focus that is suspected to be over the right occipital region.  These findings were conveyed to the primary team.

CLINICAL HISTORY:   44 year old man with recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, phenobarbital, and Topamax.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizure.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software and nurses' alarm noted events were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well-defined posterior dominant rhythm of 9 to 10 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is a normal amount of frontocentral beta.  Activating procedures were not performed.  The patient is recorded in wakefulness and stage I, stage II, and deeper stages of sleep. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves at F7/T3 seen occasionally in wakefulness and sleep.
Focal slow waves seen at F7/T3 at 2 to 4 Hz and an amplitude of 20 to 60 microvolts. 
SEIZURES:  Seizure #1, 07/18/2012 at 10:30 a.m., complex partial seizure.  In the video, the patient suddenly places down his cell phone.  Suddenly his arms raise and he lays back.  After this, the right arm suddenly becomes dystonic and stiff.  He starts having automatic movements with the left arm, playing around with the cell phone and the sheet, and his face remains in a staring fashion.  At times he moves his mouth.  He continues this behavior during the entirety of the seizure.   
EEG:  The EEG seizure started at 10:33 a.m. and ended at 10:34 a.m. The EEG correlate to the seizure started at 7 seconds after the clinical onset.  There is rhythmic theta discharge seen maximally at the beginning at T1/F7, which evolves in frequency and increases in amplitude.  It remains mainly over the T1/F7 electrodes but it spread throughout the whole left hemisphere.  After the end of the seizure, there appears to be an asymmetry of the faster frequencies with a lack of them over the left hemisphere.  End of seizure #1. 
Seizure #2, complex partial seizure, occurred on 07/19/2012, starting at 2:48:57, ending at 2:49:52.  Video behavior:  While in bed the patient starts blinking and moving his mouth.  Suddenly his right arm becomes dystonic and his right eye shuts down and appears to twitch slightly while he continues to move his mouth.  Seconds later, he starts having rhythmic automatisms of the left hand and playing with the sheet.  He continues this behavior until the end of the seizure.   
EEG:  About 10 seconds after clinical onset, the EEG reveals well-formed rhythmic 7 Hz theta discharge, maximal over the T1/F7 electrode, which is slightly obscured by muscle artifact which appears to continue to evolve in frequency and increase in amplitude over the left hemisphere.  The seizure remains on the left hemisphere.  It does not cross to the right.  After the end of the seizure, there appear to be a lot of faster frequencies over that left hemisphere and not noticed over the right.  The seizure lasts for 55 seconds.  End of seizure #2. 
Seizure #3, electrographic seizures.  During the record, he had about 4 distinct electrographic seizures which were comprised of rhythmic runs of F7/T3 theta discharge that appeared to increase slightly in amplitude and then suddenly stop.  These 4 events lasted as long as 4 minutes.  During them, in the video there is no clear clinical correlate.  End of seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
1.  Left anterior temporal sharp waves and slowing. 
2.  Complex partial seizures and multiple electrographic seizures.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This long-term EEG is diagnostic of localization related epilepsy with seizures arising from the left anterior temporal region.

It remains mainly over the T1/F7 electrodes but it spread throughout the whole left hemisphere.  After the end of the seizure, there appears to be an asymmetry of the faster frequencies with a lack of them over the left hemisphere.  End of seizure #1. 
Seizure #2, complex partial seizure, occurred on 07/19/2012, starting at 2:48:57, ending at 2:49:52.  Video behavior:  While in bed the patient starts blinking and moving his mouth.  Suddenly his right arm becomes dystonic and his right eye shuts down and appears to twitch slightly while he continues to move his mouth.  Seconds later, he starts having rhythmic automatisms of the left hand and playing with the sheet.  He continues this behavior until the end of the seizure.   
EEG:  About 10 seconds after clinical onset, the EEG reveals well-formed rhythmic 7 Hz theta discharge, maximal over the T1/F7 electrode, which is slightly obscured by muscle artifact which appears to continue to evolve in frequency and increase in amplitude over the left hemisphere.  The seizure remains on the left hemisphere.  It does not cross to the right.  After the end of the seizure, there appear to be a lot of faster frequencies over that left hemisphere and not noticed over the right.  The seizure lasts for 55 seconds.  End of seizure #2. 
Seizure #3, electrographic seizures.  During the record, he had about 4 distinct electrographic seizures which were comprised of rhythmic runs of F7/T3 theta discharge that appeared to increase slightly in amplitude and then suddenly stop.  These 4 events lasted as long as 4 minutes.  During them, in the video there is no clear clinical correlate.  End of seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
1.  Left anterior temporal sharp waves and slowing. 
2.  Complex partial seizures and multiple electrographic seizures.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This long-term EEG is diagnostic of localization related epilepsy with seizures arising from the left anterior temporal region.


CLINICAL HISTORY:   44 year old man with recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, phenobarbital, and Topamax.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizure.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software and nurses' alarm noted events were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well-defined posterior dominant rhythm of 9 to 10 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is a normal amount of frontocentral beta.  Activating procedures were not performed.  The patient is recorded in wakefulness and stage I, stage II, and deeper stages of sleep. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves at F7/T3 seen occasionally in wakefulness and sleep.
Focal slow waves seen at F7/T3 at 2 to 4 Hz and an amplitude of 20 to 60 microvolts. 
SEIZURES:  Seizure #1, 07/18/2012 at 10:30 a.m., complex partial seizure.  In the video, the patient suddenly places down his cell phone.  Suddenly his arms raise and he lays back.  After this, the right arm suddenly becomes dystonic and stiff.  He starts having automatic movements with the left arm, playing around with the cell phone and the sheet, and his face remains in a staring fashion.  At times he moves his mouth.  He continues this behavior during the entirety of the seizure.   
EEG:  The EEG seizure started at 10:33 a.m. and ended at 10:34 a.m. The EEG correlate to the seizure started at 7 seconds after the clinical onset.  There is rhythmic theta discharge seen maximally at the beginning at T1/F7, which evolves in frequency and increases in amplitude.  It remains mainly over the T1/F7 electrodes but it spread throughout the whole left hemisphere.  After the end of the seizure, there appears to be an asymmetry of the faster frequencies with a lack of them over the left hemisphere.  End of seizure #1. 
Seizure #2, complex partial seizure, occurred on 07/19/2012, starting at 2:48:57, ending at 2:49:52.  Video behavior:  While in bed the patient starts blinking and moving his mouth.  Suddenly his right arm becomes dystonic and his right eye shuts down and appears to twitch slightly while he continues to move his mouth.  Seconds later, he starts having rhythmic automatisms of the left hand and playing with the sheet.  He continues this behavior until the end of the seizure.   
EEG:  About 10 seconds after clinical onset, the EEG reveals well-formed rhythmic 7 Hz theta discharge, maximal over the T1/F7 electrode, which is slightly obscured by muscle artifact which appears to continue to evolve in frequency and increase in amplitude over the left hemisphere.  The seizure remains on the left hemisphere.  It does not cross to the right.  After the end of the seizure, there appear to be a lot of faster frequencies over that left hemisphere and not noticed over the right.  The seizure lasts for 55 seconds.  End of seizure #2. 
Seizure #3, electrographic seizures.  During the record, he had about 4 distinct electrographic seizures which were comprised of rhythmic runs of F7/T3 theta discharge that appeared to increase slightly in amplitude and then suddenly stop.  These 4 events lasted as long as 4 minutes.  During them, in the video there is no clear clinical correlate.  End of seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
1.  Left anterior temporal sharp waves and slowing. 
2.  Complex partial seizures and multiple electrographic seizures.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This long-term EEG is diagnostic of localization related epilepsy with seizures arising from the left anterior temporal region.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  58 year old female with resection of a left frontal mass and history of seizures, now status post surgery with recurrent seizures.  She also has a past medical history of bipolar, depression, hypertension, and diabetes.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, Zonegran.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection as well as seizure detection software as well as patient's noted and nurse's noted events were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with a posterior dominant rhythm that at best reaches 5 Hz which is not reactive to eye opening.  There is no normal frontocentral beta.  There are at times appears to be stage changes and appears to go into sleep, but the normal sleep architecture is not usually seen.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen at F8/T4 and independently at F7/T3, a little bit more frequently on the left. 
Focal slowing is seen at F8/T4 and at F7/T3 at 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.
There is focal slowing also seen at C3/CZ continuously at 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-70 microvolts.
SEIZURES:  During the recording and especially particularly in the first 2 hours, there were multiple seizures about 10 noted originating with a rhythmic theta discharge seen at F7/T3 increasing in frequency and decreasing in amplitude at times ping-ponging to the right anterior temporal region and sometimes remaining in the same region, lasting for 30-45 seconds.  During these episodes, the patient appears to have right arm stiffening and mouth movements as well as staring unresponsively.  After the first 2 hours of the recording, there were only 2 seizures seen in the rest of the recording for about 15 hours.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Multiple seizures arising from the left anterior temporal region. 
Bi-temporal sharp waves. 
Background slowing and left central focal slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of multiple seizures arising from the left temporal region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal structural dysfunction and irritability arising from both anterior temporal lobes which has a high correlation for seizures arising from either region.  Lastly, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the left central region which is consistent with the known tumor resection.  In addition to all this, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific with regards to etiology, but may be due at least in part to medication effect.  These findings were communicated to the primary team.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 43-year-old man with history of seizures after stroke, now with recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Lamictal.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with the addition of anterior temple single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  After 8 p.m., some of the central leads were lost and by 11 p.m., all the electrodes were lost.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There were limited waveforms, as there appears to be posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 8 Hz which is seen bilaterally and reactive to eye opening.  There is an increased amount of beta seen throughout the recording.
  
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Some intermittent focal slowing can be seen over the right hemisphere at T4/C4.
SEIZURES:  At 5 p.m., the patient had a seizure where during the video, although not clearly seen, it is apparent that the left side of his body was twitching.  However, this is not clearly seen as the video was focused on the right side of his body.  In the EEG, there is muscle artifact seen maximally over the left hemisphere in a rhythmic high-frequency fashion.  However, it is not clear if there is any appreciable discharge over the right hemisphere as there is too much muscle artifact and before the seizures started, the patient was eating which makes it even more difficult to localize or clearly identify a seizure onset.  The seizure lasted for the duration of approximately 1 minute, 45 seconds.  After the seizure, the patient was back to baseline.  No other seizures were seen.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to single seizure of unclear onset, likely arising from the right hemisphere and focal slowing in the right temporal region.  Clinical correlation is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with seizure likely arising from the right hemisphere.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the right mid temporal region which may be due to the previous infarct.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  Epilepsy, multiple strokes in the right PCA territory.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous video EEG was performed in the unit using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.  The patient had one episode on the morning of November 9, 2012 with impaired speech and difficulty using the left arm.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  Random wakefulness and sleep:  In wakefulness there are sections of the record with a relatively symmetric background and subtle, if any, focal slowing in the right hemisphere.  The alpha rhythm on the right is starting to reemerge.  Subtle focal slowing is noted in the right central parietal region.   
		
Occasional wicket is noted as the patient drifts off to sleep.   
There are a number of transients noted in the transition to sleep which have a mitten like appearance, but no definite spike and wave complexes.   
At 9:10 p.m. on November 8, 2012 there is a burst of rhythmic activity from the right hemisphere.  There is no push button or clinical information to suggest that this was considered to be a seizure. 
The sleep section of the record demonstrates disrupted sleep.  There are a number of mitten-like discharges which may represent subtle, spike and wave complexes.   
Seizures:  Time 7:00 a.m.  The clinical team was aware of the seizure, but did not press the button.  By 7:00 a.m. there is some subtle activity with a bit more rhythmic slowing emanating from the right hemisphere.  By 7:06 a.m. more clear, rhythmic slowing is noted from the right hemisphere.  The physician comes in to examine the patient.  There is a great deal of muscle and movement artifact during the event.  The clinical symptoms are associated with rhythmic activity, which is relatively prolonged.  In addition, the patient is moving about quite a bit.  The activity lasts until 7:56 and then disappears.   
This discharge was associated with a clinical change in the patient's state and should be considered and epileptiform seizure.
INTERIM IMPRESSION/CLINICAL CORRELATION:  Focal slowing is noted from the right, congruent with a stroke, but there was a clinical seizure at approximately 7:00 a.m., which was relatively prolonged with rhythmic activity.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  43 year old male with melanoma, mets, and status post brain surgery who presented with a generalized tonic clonic seizure.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote.
REASON FOR EEG:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed  in lab using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with an asymmetry in the frontal central beta seen more on the right.  There is no normal posterior dominant rhythm of 8 Hz to 9 Hz is seen over the left hemisphere.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:
Focal sharp waves and slowing are seen at T4/F8 continuously throughout the recording with associated breech.  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz are seen throughout the recording.  In addition, there is increased diffuse beta seen at times. 
SEIZURES:  At times during the recording the frontal sharp waves which appear to become more rhythmic in a pattern of 1-3 Hz and at times evolve to faster frequencies and then stop and start.  This is most consistent with small electrographic seizures.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:  
Right anterior temporal sharp waves and slow waves. 
Intermittent electrographic procedures in the setting of a breech.  
Diffuse slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy, with electrographic seizures arising from the right anterior temporal region.  There is also evidence of focal structural dysfunction and irritability, which is 
consistent with a known tumor.  These findings were communicated to the team as soon as the EEG was performed.

Status post radiation and chemotherapy in 2012.MEDICATIONS: Ativan, Dilantin, Cogentin INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at the bedside in the ICU standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel EKG.  This is a 40-minute EEG in a confused individual.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not possible.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There are some sections of the record particularly when the patient is stimulated where the EEG demonstrates a 9 Hz alpha rhythm on the right.  There is occasionally an alpha rhythm identified on the left.  Relevant features for this EEG include continuous, arrhythmic delta activity in the left hemisphere, maximum frontotemporal with a generous field of spread and disruption of faster frequency activity throughout the left frontocentral and frontotemporal region.  A breach rhythm was identified.  Intermittent bursts of paroxysmal fast activity were noted in the left frontocentral region with spread to the midline.   This individual had several behaviors including head turning to the left and staring.  The individual was confused throughout.   During this EEG, there appeared to have been 2 electrographic seizures without clear clinical correlate and without clear evidence of this other stereotypic behavior which was described.  The patient seemed to stare vacantly, but on 1 occasion was mumbling and squeezing fingers.  Seizure onset and offset is difficult to determine, but the discharges seemed to build up at 9:04 and again less clearly at 9:18.  Just prior to the 9:18, 9:19 discharge the epileptiform activity with paroxysmal periodic sharply contoured delta at T3 had been abating.   Ativan was given which clearly decreased both the bursts of paroxysmal fast activity in the left frontal region and the paroxysmal sharply contoured activity in the left frontotemporal region.  By the end of the EEG, occasional isolated sharp waves maximum at F3 were identified.   Heart rate 96 BPM.IMPRESSION:  1. Although seizure onset and offset were difficult to determine, this EEG does demonstrate poorly defined seizures.2. Focal slowing in the left frontocentral region.3. Left frontocentral paroxysmal fast activity.4. Sharp waves noted in the left hemisphere including T3 and F3.CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG supports a late form of status epilepticus or a very irritative pattern.  The results were reported to the team and EEG monitoring was initiated.CLINICAL HISTORY: 53 year old female with Hypoglycemic and anoxic

The patient is unable to speak during the seizure and in the postictal period he nods his head from side to side.  He has a relatively lengthy postictal period including almost all the way to the end of the record.  Over time, he becomes increasingly able to speak, although initially he answers all questions with “uhm-uhm” noise.  Over time, he can answer questions, but has difficulty performing simple tasks.  
HR:  94 BPM.

Left temporal seizure, relatively well characterized by scalp EEG.
Left temporal focal slowing with rare sharp waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION: Diagnostic of a partial mechanism for epilepsy.
