The activity is seen in the first ten seconds of the EEG and occurs in wakefulness.  The recording that follows includes a somewhat drowsy pattern.  There is intermittent occipitally predominant rhythmic delta activity, which is sometimes a bit more prominent on the left than the right. 
The first well-defined seizure with seizure onset is at 13:08:26.  The child is sitting comfortably.  As is the case with the additional awake seizures, he raises his arms and does not speak.  Testing is performed.  The child speaks afterwards.  He doesn’t seem to be entirely out of it during the spells.  The EEG demonstrates a generalized, 3 to 4 Hz spike and slow wave complex.  This very first spell seems to be a bit more remarkable in the left compared to the right.  There seems to be a little sharp wave that precedes on the left prior to the generalized discharge. The tech started hyperventilation when the first event occurred.  She gives the patient a code word during the first spell and responsiveness is impaired.  During the second seizure at 13:10:06, the patient is given a color.  Once again, he is noted to just very gently raise his arms above his head.  This discharge is similar to the previous with frontally predominant 3 to 5 Hz spike and slow wave activity and poly spike and slow wave activity with abrupt cessation when the event is over.  This one seems to be more synchronous with fast activity seen in the frontal regions, but again has just the slightest bit of focality to the left at the onset.  The third seizure occurs at 13:14:00 following hyperventilation.  This seizure is shorter (most seizures are 20 seconds in duration, this one is 14 seconds).  He lifts his leg gently and puts it back down, but the previously described behavior was not seen with the arms.  Additional seizures including that at 13:16:06 occur when the child is asleep.  The epileptiform activity is essentially identical with a very high amplitude, at times greater than 200 msc spike and slow wave discharges.  This is bilateral, but again there is a bit more intense fast activity in the left frontal region than the right.  Following this, the patient is drowsy but not entirely asleep, although there is some stage II sleep with vertex waves, K complexes, and spindles.  In the drowsy pattern, there is a bit of fast activity in the frontocentral regions.  The fifth seizure is at 13:18:36 with the patient drifting off to sleep.  No clinical signs are seen for this drowsy individual, but testing is not performed with the intention to let the child fall asleep.  Immediately after the discharge, a vertex wave can be seen supporting the appearance of this activity as the child drifts off to sleep.  Vertex waves and spindles are seen in stage II sleep.  The sixth seizure is recorded in stage II sleep at 13:22:46, ending at 13:32:56.  No clinical signs or symptoms are noted with this.  Additional, brief or less than 10 second discharges are also seen in sleep.  There are a handful of poly spike discharges noted in this sleep record, but mostly it is the longer discharges.  An additional discharge is observed at 13:27:36, which seems to slightly disrupt sleep.
Stimulation does not elicit a photoparoxysmal response.
HR:  100 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Spontaneous and hyperventilation-induced frontally predominant bursts of generalized spike and slow wave activity.
Rare bursts of fast activity from the left frontal region in sleep. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: On the whole, this EEG is most supportive of a primary generalized mechanism for epilepsy with frontally predominant spike and slow wave activity.  There are some features raising the possibility of a secondarily generalized mechanism including the left frontal activity seen at the onset of this.  This may represent the orientation of the dipole for this individual.  The seizures that occurred were relatively typical of absence seizures.  Correlation with clinical history and imaging is appropriate.  
Of note, the attending physician of record was notified during the EEG. 


CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 61-year-old woman with a right PCA infarct with ischemia involving the anterior temporal structures with status epilepticus. 
MEDICATIONS: Dilantin, ?others, Ativan, Etomadate.
INTRODUCTION: Continuous video EEG is performed at the bedside in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG. At least in the initial portions of the record, where clinical comments are available, the patient is described as clinically seizing with involuntary movements of the left hand.  She is intubated and poorly responsive.  In the later portions of the record, clinical correlates of the seizures include subtle jerking of the left hand against the bed rail and subtle movements of the patient’s head.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: This EEG demonstrates ongoing, repetitive seizures emanating from the right hemisphere.  The seizures start as the EEG opens with sharply contoured theta frequency activity and sharp waves maximum from the right frontotemporal region with clear frequency evolution.  The seizures are approximately 45 seconds, but actual seizure onset and off set is difficult and seizures occur within a minute at the end of the first seizure.  In the more vigorous seizures, the patient has some movement of the left foot.  In some of them, there is simply tremulousness of the left.  As the recording continues, while there is no mention of a change in medications for this patient, the background becomes a bit more discontinuous and a bit more suppressed.  There is ongoing epileptiform activity, however, with fast activity and sharp waves seen in what is otherwise a somewhat suboptimal EEG from a technical perspective with some impedance artifact, ongoing epileptiform discharges and frequency evolution compatible with clinical seizures lasting approximately 45 to 60 seconds can be seen.  There is also some slight discontinuity in the record.  There is no mention of stimulation of the patient throughout the EEG.  It cannot be determined from this EEG if testing was performed.  
HR:  124 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Status epilepticus emanating from the right hemisphere.
Marked background suppression from the left.
Evidence of discontinuity towards the close of the record. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: This EEG is indicative of a severe form of status epilepticus.  These results might be more helpful if additional clinical information including medications were known. 
Long-Term Monitoring - December 24, 2004 to December 25, 2004
MEDICATIONS: Dilantin and Ativan.
INTRODUCTION: Digital video EEG is performed at the bedside in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG. There are no comments regarding changes in medications for this patient.  There is a comment that the patient may have renal failure.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: Clinically, the patient remains comatose and intubated.  Review of the video does not discern involuntary movements in association with epileptiform activity.  However, although the EEG demonstrates an intermittent burst suppression, in the portions of the EEG that are continuous the patient demonstrates seizures characterized by fast activity and sharp waves from the right hemisphere, particularly the right anterior temporal region.  The duration of the events is 45 to 100 seconds, in some cases with a brief period of suppression.  Towards the close of the record on the 25th, the EEG is more continuous, but there is ongoing sharp waves from the right hemisphere, particularly the right anterior temporal with some frequency evolution seen at that time.  At the close of the record, the EEG is no longer in burst suppression and there is more of a pattern of uniform theta.  An electrographic seizure is identified at 7:43 and the recording ends at 8:08 suggesting that the overall frequency of seizures has decreased from nearly continuous to at least hourly. 
HR:  96 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Ongoing status epilepticus seen at some points in the overnight recording even in the setting of a burst suppression pattern. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: If this patient has renal failure, this may have made the epileptiform activity more difficult to control.  Additional clinical history would be important to determine whether or not the discontinuity seen in portions of the record was due to medication effect or metabolic issues. 


CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 60-year-old male with lymphoma and lifelong history of epilepsy and complex partial seizures with several complex partial seizures in the past month.  Paraparesis secondary to epidural mass. 
MEDICATIONS: Tegretol, Mysoline, Restoril, Primidone, Lidocaine, and others.
INTRODUCTION: Digital video EEG is performed in the lab using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG. Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were performed.  This patient experienced a complex partial seizure with confusion during the record. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: As the tracing opens, the background EEG is well organized.  There is a posterior dominant rhythm of 8 Hz, 30 to 50 V with a small amount of low voltage, frontocentral beta activity.  In wakefulness, there are left temporal sharp and slow wave complexes as well as associated focal slowing.  
Hyperventilation is performed early on in the record and may accentuate the bursts of focal slowing and the sharp waves.  
The patient experienced a seizure at 9:49:34.  He was resting comfortably.  The first behavioral change was that he began to demonstrate restlessness.  He then had automatisms of the left hand, while looking to the right with dystonic posturing of the right.  Afterwards, he was confused.  He was speaking but the technologist had difficulty understanding him.  He tended to use more words in Spanish than in English and was semi-cooperative, but not thinking clearly.  In terms of seizure onset, there is a high amplitude sharp and slow wave complex at 9:49:44 with a burst of focal slowing four seconds later.  The first clear ictal changes began just as he starts to fidget with left temporal theta activity of approximately 4 to 5 Hz.  This is seen at 9:50:04 and evolves over the next 14 seconds.  By 9:50:14, the activity is maximum on the left, but is clearly bilaterally.  The data is reviewed with a filter on and the epileptiform activity is most intense from the left temporal region with a phase reversal at T1/T3 but with clear bilateral spread.  At 9:50:54, when the seizure is over, there is an increase in background slowing.  This starts to resolve by 9:52:26 with the reappearance of the alpha rhythm, but again there is background slowing seen in the minutes that follow.  At 9:55 the EEG seems to have returned to baseline with intermittent focal slowing and sharp waves from the left temporal region. 
HR:  70 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Complex partial seizure with somewhat challenging localization, but maximum in the left lateral temporal electrodes. 
Intermittent focal slowing from the left temporal region.
Left anterior temporal sharp and slow waves and spike and slow wave complexes.
CLINICAL CORRELATION: This tracing is diagnostic of a partial mechanism for epilepsy.  The clinical team was contacted with these results.

	Intermittent bursts of fast activity from the right temporal region.

The background ranges from low voltage with a mixture of delta and theta to intermittent left temporal sharp waves. There are intermittent bursts of arrhythmic delta from the left.  There are also occasional, frontally predominant triphasic waves.  
The patient has multiple practice push buttons, which are not associated with a change in the EEG.  
Through the course of the night, at 1:00 AM through 3:00 AM, the nurses identify focal jerking of the left shoulder.  This occurs at 1:10 AM, 2:19 AM, 3:02 AM, and 3:08 AM.  During the episodes with the myoclonus, particularly involving the left shoulder in this patient with a right hemiparesis, while there are no definite electrical seizures, there are triphasic waves.  
At 3:03 AM, there is another push button by the nursing staff for similar activity.  At this time, there is more delta identified from the left temporal region.  The delta is sharply contoured and is relatively prolonged.  While it doesn’t clearly evolve, it represents more delta than previously seen with a vague, poorly defined seizure onset and off set. 
At the close of the record, the EEG continues to show a slow pattern with delta activity and sharp waves, most prominent in the left temporal region, as well as occasional triphasic waves.  
HR:  90 BPM, irregular.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Frequent left temporal sharp waves and focal slowing.
Triphasic waves.
A single, poorly defined seizure at 3:00 AM.
CLINICAL CORRELATION: This tracing supports a metabolic process, as well as a partial or localization-related mechanism for epilepsy.  The seizures that are identified by the EEG have weak clinical correlates.  


CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 20-year-old right-handed college student with refractory absence epilepsy.  
MEDICATIONS: Depakote and Keppra.
INTRODUCTION: Digital video EEG is performed in the unit using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG.  Multiple push button events are identified, but not for the patient’s seizures.  In addition, the patient has multiple absence spells.  She receives Keppra (Levetiracetam) during this record.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: In wakefulness, the background EEG includes a 9 Hz posterior dominant rhythm, which is symmetric and reactive to eye opening.  There are occasional bursts of isolated, frontally predominant symmetric high amplitude 5 Hz spike and slow wave activity.  The overall waking record is at the limits of normal in terms of organization for an individual of this age.  Features of stage II sleep include vertex waves, K complexes, and spindles.  In the transition to sleep, there are some briefer spike and slow wave complexes, as well as some longer bursts.  In addition, while there is no definite dyshormia, in some sections in the sleep record there is subtle sharply contoured activity in the frontal regions, but no clear asymmetry.
SEIZURES: There are multiple push buttons by the family: 17:13, 18:14, 19:19, and 20:39.  These do not appear to be seizures.  Given the language (Arabic), it is not clear what the behavior was even when reviewing the video, but these are no seizures and in some cases, when the nurse enters, the patient verbalizes in English that she is fine and that she did not experience any symptoms.
Multiple bursts of generalized spike and slow wave activity are identified.  The majority of these are 8 seconds in duration.  In some cases but not in every case it is possible to identify a clinical correlate, which includes eye blinking.  The resolution of the camera does not permit identification of the facial twitching seen in the lab.  In addition, sometimes the patient scratches her scalp during the seizures.  The minute the discharge stops, the patient is back to her usual self.  She is sitting parallel rather than facing her mother and the mother never identifies these discharges.  In the initial portions of the record, they occur approximately one per hour including 4:13:46, 6:59, 18:35, 16:09, 20:07, 37:00, and 21:40.  In addition they are seen at 7:00 AM and at 10:21 AM.  There are some computer problems and a loss of data at 3:00 AM, but it appears in wakefulness in the initial portions of the record that the activity is approximately once per hour and the limited data seen on the morning of the 1st suggests that the activity has slowed to a frequency of less than one per hour.  
At 00:55:45, the patient is sleeping.  The resolution of the video is poor, but she has a burst of frontally predominant generalized activity lasting approximately three seconds followed by an arousal without clear clinical correlate.  This is one of the infrequent times that this activity can be seen.  
HR:  96 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
Bursts of frontally predominant 3 to 6 Hz spike and slow wave activity, some of which are clinically significant. 
	
CLINICAL CORRELATION: The background EEG is at the limits of normal.  There were no definitive frontal abnormalities, but given the refractory nature of this patient’s epilepsy, if the seizures do not improve with her current treatment plan, an additional EEG recording with special electrodes would be helpful to exclude a frontal lobe process producing this epilepsy syndrome.

CLINICAL HISTORY: 19 year old left handed female postpartum female with multiple seizures 
in the past day with little in the way of postictal state. Past temporal lobectomy in 2009.

electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. Hyperventilation and photic stimulation are 
performed. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: The background EEG includes an 8.5 Hz alpha rhythm 
which is slightly slower on the left when compared to the right. There is a breech rhythm with 
amplitude activity from the left as well as some underlying theta and delta. In this sharply 
contoured record, there are occasional T3 and T5 sharp waves. The T4 sharp waves occur in 
trains. Stimulation of the patient seems to produce an increase in faster frequency activity 
bilaterally and the focal features on the left are a bit more prominent in the transition toward 
sleep. Somewhat asymptomatic vertex waves are noted and there are POSTS noted in sleep. 
The patient seems to transition in an out of sleep. Photic stimulation does elicit a scant driving 
response. In some sections of the sleep recording, with vertex waves and spindles, there are 
sections with the posterior temporal sharp waves and even some left occipital sharp waves. The 
breech rhythm is quite prominent in stage II sleep and the sharp waves range from F7 all the 
way to 01. 
HR: 78 bpm 
IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to: 
1. Focal slowing in the left temporal region with disruption of faster frequent activity as well as 
underlying slowing. 
2. Left temporal sharp waves which range in maximum from even the left occipital region to 
the left mid to anterior temporal region. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: This EEG supports a broad epileptogenic zone. Additional EEG 
recording it may be helpful to capture her frequent events at this time. 


CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 60-year-old male with a long history of alcohol withdrawal seizures and epilepsy now with subdural hematoma and recurrent seizures. 
MEDICATIONS: Keppra, Dilantin, and 6 mg of Ativan.
INTRODUCTION: Digital video EEG is performed at the bedside in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG. The patient has an episode of facial twitching just prior to EEG initiation and another clinical episode of one minutes’ duration.  
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: The background EEG demonstrates an intrahemispheric asymmetry.  Typically, the overall background is slow, but there is some alpha and theta frequency activity from the left and with arousals there is an alpha rhythm identifiable on the left.  The right hemisphere demonstrates intermittent disruption of faster frequency activity and slower arrhythmic delta activity and more of a slower, periodic complex from the right posterior quadrant.  The periodic activity is broadly formed and comes out a bit better at a slow paper speed in that the periodic discharges may be close to a second in nature with a broad distribution in the right hemisphere including the central and parietal regions.  Given the nature of the muscle activity, which occurs with the facial twitching, there isn’t a clear clinical correlate for this 60-second facial twitching with a periodic slowing from the right.  In addition, the periodic activity from the right is not at the same frequency as the facial twitching. 
This record demonstrates reactivity and variability with an intermittently more alert pattern, most remarkable from the left. 
During the EEG, the patient receives Levetiracetam and Ativan.  By the close of the record, the activity is considerably slower and the periodic activity is most remarkable in the posterior quadrant, almost with a small discrete poly spike component at P4 by the end. 
HR:  96 BPM.
	IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to:
	An electrographic seizure characterized by left facial twitching without a clear clinical correlate.
	Intrahemispheric asymmetry with intermittent, relatively prominent disruption of faster frequency activity from the right hemisphere, and a slow, periodic discharge from the right hemisphere.
	A discrete, couplet of fast activity at P4.
CLINICAL CORRELATION: The clinical event without a clear EEG signature is most compatible with an epilepsy partialis continua.  Overall, the background does seem to be improving following administration of medications.  Additional EEG monitoring may be warranted to make sure that the underlying epileptiform activity is abating.  


REASON FOR STUDY:  Recurrent seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using a standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software including nurse's note as well as reviewing the EEG was performed though the whole 24 hours and used to further evaluate the recording.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote, Dilantin, Keppra.
HISTORY:  A 25-year-old man with seizure, right-sided after left brain tumor resection now with recurrent multiple seizures refractory to medications and status epilepticus.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to background that has a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 8.5 Hz over the right hemisphere at times and is reactive to eye opening.  However, over the left hemisphere, there is no posterior dominant rhythm, no clear normal frontocentral beta seen.  The patient appears to have wakefulness and sleep stage changes with some normal sleep architecture.  Activating procedures were not performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Continuous focal slowing seen over the left hemisphere, maximal at T3, a frequency of 2-4 Hz, amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.  There is a symmetric amplitude over this hemisphere compared to the rest consistent with a breach over the left hemisphere.
SEIZURES:  During the first 2 hours of the recording, there appeared to be intermittent rhythmic discharges at 1 Hz starting and stopping suddenly arising from the left occipital region maximal at 01 with some T5/ P3 component.  This are sometimes prolong, lasting for more than 40 seconds and consisting with electrographic seizures.  They were seen for about a period of 2 hours and no longer seen in the rest of the recording.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left hemisphere focal slowing, maximal in the mid temporal region in addition to multiple electrographic seizures arising from the left occipital and parietal region occurring mainly at the beginning of the recording.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence multiple electrographic seizures arising from the left occipital and parietal region which improves after the first 4 hours in the morning.   In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction over the left hemisphere, maximal at the mid temporal region, which is consistent with the known tumor resection.  These findings were reported to the primary team.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 25-year-old man with a history of left parietal tumor resected, status post radiation and recent stroke in addition to possible vasculitis, presents with recurrent confusion. EEG is to rule out electrographic status epilepticus.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat and Depakote.
INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The EEG reveals a posterior dominant rhythm of 9 Hz which is best seen over the right hemisphere.  There is some frontal central beta seen best over the right hemisphere, at times seen over the left.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Some normal sleep architecture is seen.   
Abnormal discharges:
Focal slow waves seen continuously over P3 and at times T3 at a frequency of 2-4 Hz.  Amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.  Focal sharp waves are seen at O1 independently occasionally.   
Seizures:
The patient had during this recording 2 electrographic seizures.  The first one occurring at 7:18 p.m. until 7:28 p.m. lasting for 10 minutes.  It appears to originate at O1 with rhythmic sharp waves in the theta frequency, that increasing amplitude and frequency and appeared to spread slightly to O2 but remained mainly in the occipital region.  During the event the patient moves around but no other clear clinical features can be seen in the video.  At 12:30 a.m. the patient had another seizure lasting 5 minutes.  This time the patient again was in the bed and no clinical seizures were seen.  In the EEG there was again in O1 an electrographic seizure that began with a discharge of spikes in the alpha frequency that continued to evolve to the theta frequency and increasing amplitude and then suddenly stopped. This one was spread less through the occipital region and stayed mainly at O1.  During the rest of the night no more seizures were seen until and this EEG was reviewed until 8:00 in the morning.   
Heart rate.  72.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to 2 electrographic seizures arising from the left occipital region, in addition to focal slowing seen continuously over the left hemisphere maximal in the parietal and temporal region.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals or is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with 2 seizures arising from the left occipital region.  In addition, there is no evidence of focal structural cerebral dysfunction in the left hemisphere maximum in the parietal and temporal region which may be related to the known tumor resection.  These findings were communicated to the primary team as they occurred.


DATE OF STUDY:  08/15/2013 to 08/16/2013, for a total of 24 hours.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 25-year-old man with a history of a past tumor resection, chemotherapy and radiation therapy, and previous history of localization related epilepsy who returns with multiple seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, Dilantin, Depakote.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection as well as seizure detection software as well as nurses' notes were all used in addition to reviewing the EEG to make this report.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record begins with a background that is slightly slow, particularly the posterior dominant rhythm appears to be mainly in the theta frequency, about 7 Hz, and it is best seen over the right hemisphere.  There is some frontal beta that starts to be seen some hours into the recording which continues to improve as well as the background increases in frequency.  Some sleep architecture is seen, and state changes are noted.  No activating procedures were performed. 
Abnormal discharges:
Focal slow waves seen continuously over the left hemisphere, maximal at F3/C3 as well as T5/P3 at a frequency of 2 to 4 Hz and amplitude of 20 to 60 microvolts.  This slowing is seen continuously throughout the recording.
Generalized slowing at 5 to 7 Hz, seen at early onset of the recording.  It appears to improve slowly throughout the lasts hours of the recording. 
Seizures:  At 10:09 p.m., there appears to be an occipital seizure commencing at O1 with intermittent theta frequency sharp waves that continue to increase in frequency and amplitude but remain over the left occipital hemisphere and continue to discharge for about 20 minutes.  During this time there appears to be no clinical correlate to the patient seen in the video. 
Heart rate 90.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to a single left occipital electrographic seizure, in addition to continuous left hemispheric slowing and mild generalized slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with a single electrographic seizure arising from the left occipital region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral structural dysfunction in the left hemisphere, maximal in the central frontal region, which is consistent with the known tumor resection.  Lastly, there is evidence of mild diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which may be due, at least in part, to medication effect.  These findings were reported continuously to the primary team.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  Patient is a 25-year-old man with a history of a left parieto-occipital tumor removal at the age of 12, who now presents with worsening aphasia and right hemiparesis.
MEDICATIONS:  Current medications include Depakote, Vimpat, phenobarbital, clonazepam, and 8 mg of Versed total at around 12 p.m. and an extra dose of Depakote 500 mg administered at approximately 1 a.m.
CONDITIONS OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10/20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.  The recording was reviewed intermittently by the attending physician during the recording epoch.  Medical staff notes and the nursing notes were reviewed.  The patient was equipped with the event button.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORDING:  There is right-to-left interhemispheric asymmetry with slower waveforms at high amplitudes over the left hemisphere.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the right hemisphere consists of a low amplitude 9 Hz alpha activity that attenuates with eyes opening.  There is an anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient over the right.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the left hemisphere consists of 8-9 Hz activity.  There is no clear anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient over the left hemisphere.  Additional theta and delta activity is observed over the left hemisphere.  Delta activity is especially pronounced anteriorly.  Degree of diffuse theta and delta increases after approximately 12 p.m.  This increase is transient, and is related to  administration of 8 mg of Versed.  A prolonged left occipital electrographic seizure is observed.  It starts at approximately 11:35 a.m. and ends at approximately 12:30 p.m.  Seizure stops after initiation of 8 mg of Versed total seizures start over the left occipital region and gradually spreads throughout the left hemisphere with reflection towards the right hemisphere.  After administration of Versed, seizure settles over the left occipital region and stays there until approximately 12:30 p.m.   Clinically seizure is characterized by decreased alertness as well as rhythmic little lateral horizontal nystagmus to the right.
FINDINGS:  
A single prolonged approximately 1 hour focal seizure that starts over the left occipital region.  Seizure stops after 8 mg of Versed.  
Mild diffuse slowing, most especially pronounced after Versed administration.  
Continuous focal moderate left hemispheric slowing, most pronounced anteriorly.
IMPRESSION:  This is an abnormal 24-hour continuous audio/video EEG monitoring due to continuous moderate left hemispheric slowing most pronounced anteriorly and a single prolonged left occipital quadrant seizure.  Focal slowing indicates focal cerebral dysfunction, likely structural in etiology and is consistent with patient's history of a prior tumor resection from the left posterior occipital region.  Slowing is also at least partially related to the postictal phenomenon.  There is an epileptogenic focus emanating from the left occipital region.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 25-year-old man with a history of a left parieto-occipital tumor removed at the age of 12, who now presents with recurrent left occipital seizures.  The patient subsequently found to have a small left occipital bleed.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, Depakote and phenobarbital and 2 mg of Ativan this a.m.
CONDITION OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed continuously.  Spike detector and seizure detector were activated.  Medical staff notes and nursing staff notes were reviewed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORDING:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background consists of a mixture of low amplitude beta activity admixed with theta and delta.  Delta activity is more pronounced anteriorly especially over the left hemisphere.  Additional polymorphic delta is noted over the left hemisphere.  No normal sleep architecture is observed, but periods of quiescence are noted.  On 08/23/2013 between approximately 6:50 a.m. until approximately 7:59 a.m., approximately 8 electrographic seizures were observed.  They last from about 1 minute to 2-1/2 minutes.  One more seizure is observed after Ativan administration.  The seizures started with very low amplitude fast activity over the left occipital region that rapidly spread throughout the left hemisphere, decrease in frequency and increasing in amplitude.  As the seizure progresses, frequency of rhythmic activity decreases to approximately 3 Hz and gradually stops.  Clinically seizures are associated with rhythmic lateral gaze nystagmus to the right.
FINDINGS:  
Moderate diffuse background slowing.
Focal left hemispheric slowing.
Excess beta.
Multiple electrographic seizures between approximately 7:00 a.m. and 8 a.m. on 08/23/2013 that is improved with Ativan.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 24-hour continuous audio/video EEG monitoring due to moderate diffuse slowing, focal left hemispheric slowing, and multiple focal electrographic seizures that occur within 1 hour at the end of the recording epoch.  Diffuse slowing indicates etiologically nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction, that is in this case likely related to medication effect.  Focal left hemispheric slowing indicates focal cerebral dysfunction likely structural in etiology and at least partially related to postictal phenomenon.  Excessive beta could be secondary to medication effect such as benzodiazepines __________  There is an epileptogenic focus over the left occipital region and is consistent with a history of recent left occipital region bleed.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology primary team.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 25-year-old man with history of a left cerebellar tumor resection status post chemo and radiation, and epilepsy secondary to the tumor, presents with recurrent seizures with right-sided shaking and confusion, as well as paralysis.

MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Tylenol, Lovenox, Dilantin and clindamycin.

INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while awake.  Activating procedures were not performed.

DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a posterior dominant rhythm that can only be seen over the right hemisphere that reaches 8.5 Hz.  There is some frontocentral beta seen over the right hemisphere only.  The patient was recorded only in wakefulness.  Activating procedures were not performed.

HEART RATE:  74.

ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Focal slow wave seen continuously over the left hemisphere maximal at T3 at a frequency of 1-2 Hz and an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.  This is seen continuously throughout the recording.  At times, it becomes very monomorphic.  In addition, over the anterior electrodes, there appears to be an intermixed frontal very sharply contoured theta that appears in bursts of about 1 second, stops and starts again, occurring intermittently.

SEIZURES:  During the recording, the patient had seizures that began with his eyes turning to the right followed by a burst of head movement to the right, then his right arm became tonic and stretched tonically in front of him, followed by some dystonic posturing of the right hand and trembling while the left arm became flexed at the elbow in front of him making a sign of "4".  Afterward, the patient slowly developed clonic movements and the seizure slowly subsided as the clonic movements decreased in frequency and amplitude. Last ictal jerk was on the right arm.

EEG:  During this episode, the EEG at onset revealed the rhythmic left hemispheric slowing, but no clear onset could be ascertained for the seizure.  The seizure was quickly masked by muscle artifact and there appeared to be some prefrontal sharps in between the muscle artifact.  After the event, there appears to be focal remaining slowing over the left hemisphere and the right hemisphere appears to have almost normal theta to alpha rhythm.

IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to secondary generalized tonic-clonic seizures likely arising from the left frontal region, based on semiology and EEG, in addition to continuous focal slowing over the left hemisphere maximal in the mid temporal region.


CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization-related epilepsy with seizures arising from the left hemisphere likely frontal region, in addition to revealing focal structural cerebral dysfunction from the left hemisphere maximal in the mid temporal region which is consistent with the known lesions.  These findings were reported to the primary team as they occurred.






CLINICAL HISTORY:  
A 25-year-old man with previous left frontal tumor removal, status post radiation and chemo, presents with recurrent seizures and right hemiparesis, now with recurrent secondary generalized tonic clonic seizures and status epilepticus.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Tylenol, Lovenox, Dilantin, Depakote and phenobarbital.
INTRODUCTION:  
A long term EEG was performed using a standard 10/20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode. The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep. Activating procedures were not performed. Continuous spike detection as well as seizure detection software, as well as nurse's notes and patient's events were used to review the EEG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  
The record overall has a right posterior dominant rhythm that can be seen over the right hemisphere that reaches 8.5 Hz, but not over the left hemisphere. There is normal beta over the right hemisphere only. Patient appears to go into different stages of sleep, but no normal sleep architecture is clearly observed. No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal slow waves are seen continuously over the left hemisphere, maximal at T3. These can become very monomorphic and rhythmic with at times an overlying frontal theta that is very sharply contoured and appearing in bursts of one second, occurring intermittently. Overall, this pattern seems to improve by the later hours of the recording and it appears to be less monomorphic and none of that frontal theta is seen. There is some intermittent faster frequency seen at the end of the recording.
SEIZURES:  
The patient had three seizures during the recording. The first occurred at 8:44 p.m., the second one occurred at 10:30 p.m., and the last one occurred at 1:13 in the morning. Although the video was not clear on all of them, the patient first appears to turn his head to the right, with his eyes twitching in a nystagmoid fashion with a faster component towards the right, and this movement of the head is clearly versive. This is followed by right arm extension and left arm flexion and the sign of four, while afterwards generalizing and having clonic movements. The seizures usually last a minute and 40 seconds to two minutes. During all three seizures, the onset of the seizure in the EEG is not clear, as it rapidly obscured by muscle artifact, but during the seizure there appears to be some left prefrontal sharp waves seen.
IMPRESSION:  
Abnormal EEG due to three secondary generalized seizures, likely arising from the left frontal region based on semiology, in addition to left hemispheric continuous focal slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  
This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy, now with status epilepticus with multiple seizures arising from the left hemisphere, likely frontal region, based on semiology. In addition to revealing evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the left hemisphere, maximal in the
temporal region which is consistent with a known injury. These findings were all reported to the primary team as they occurred.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This patient is a 25-year-old man with a history of left parietal tumor resection now with recurrent small hemorrhages and refractory seizures.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
MEDICATIONS:  Versed, Depakote, Vimpat, and phenobarbital.
INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed from 8/23/2013 to 8/24/2013 for a total of 24 hours using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and a single lead EKG electrode.   The patient was recorded while unresponsive and intubated.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software in relation to nurses' notes, alarms, as well as review of the EEG as well as primary team notes was used to review the EEG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There is no normal slope architecture.  No normal stage changes.  There is some reactivity.  Overall there is diffuse delta background at 2-4 Hz.  During the day the patient is placed on Versed and the EEG continues to be slow but there is an overriding of beta discharge mainly in the frontal region sometimes as the electroencephalogram continues appears to be slightly more diffuse.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.
SEIZURES:  The patient had a total of 7 seizures, the first 1 in this 24-hour period occurring at 11:00 in the morning.  The great majority of them lasted for a period of 2-3 minutes.  They all appeared to start with a fairly generalized alpha discharge at 11 Hz that settles more prominently at the T5/O1 electrode and then remains at the O1 electrode mainly.  Of note, there is some discharged through P3 and then continues to decrease in frequency and increasing amplitude and then slowly becomes slower voltages and small spikes are seen on the occipital region until it stops. There is postictal slowing noted heavily over the entire left hemisphere.  No clear clinical correlation was reported with these seizures specifically.
HEART RATE:  96.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left occipital seizures as well as generalized swelling.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of status epilepticus with multiple seizures arising from the left occipital hemisphere.  There is evidence of generalized diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is at least in part due to medication affect.  Please note that for the past 19 hours the patient has not had any more seizures while on a Versed drip.  These findings were communicated to the primary team.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 61-year-old man with history of alcohol abuse with new onset focal seizures with right upper extremity shaking, eyes deviating to the left.MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin.INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed from October 20, 2012 to October 22, 2012 using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG.  The patient was recorded during responsiveness.  Activating procedures were not performed.TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed.  Overall there is a generalized delta with at times overriding bursts of theta bursts.  At times there is also slight diffuse attenuation. Abnormal discharges:1. Focal sharp waves seen continuously with a frontal predominance at F7/F3 that appear to move more posteriorly, more centrally, and then more posteriorly towards C3 and P3 at a frequency of 1-2 Hz rhythmically and monomorphically consistent with periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLED's).  2. Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-40 mV seen continuously throughout the recording.  At times there is an overriding beta that occurs infrequently and also some diffuse burst suppression for less than 1 to 2 seconds. Seizures:1. On 10/21/2012 at 9:43am and on 10/22/2012 at 4:01am there were seizures seen on the EEG with a sudden increase in frequency to a beta discharge of about 13-14 Hz maximally over the left frontal F3 electrode and C3 central electrode which continues to increase in frequency and in about a minute there appears to be an electrographic correlate, a clinical correlate, with right facial twitching.  This discharge continues for about 1-2 minutes and then suddenly stops and subsides.  It remains mainly over the left hemisphere and does not appear to spread to the right.  2. On 10/22/2012 at 8:24 a.m. the patient develops a similar rhythmic beta discharge which increases in frequency and appears to settle over the left frontal (F3) and moves centrally (C3) region, however, remaining only over the left hemisphere.  This seizures lasts about 1 minute and has no clinical correlate to it.IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left frontal central seizures, two with a focal motor correlate in the right face, as well as one electrographic, in addition to left frontal central PLED's and diffuse slowing.CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG revealed 3 left frontal central seizures, two with a focal motor correlate and one electrographic seizure, which is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy.  In addition, there is evidence of left frontal central acute structural dysfunction and irritability.  Lastly, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific, but maybe due at least in part to medication effect. These findings were reported to the primary team after review of the EEG.REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 61-year-old man with history of alcohol abuse with new onset focal seizures with right upper extremity shaking, eyes deviating to the left.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin.
INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed from October 20, 2012 to October 22, 2012 using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG.  The patient was recorded during responsiveness.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed.  Overall there is a generalized delta with at times overriding bursts of theta bursts.  At times there is also slight diffuse attenuation. 
Abnormal discharges:
Focal sharp waves seen continuously with a frontal predominance at F7/F3 that appear to move more posteriorly, more centrally, and then more posteriorly towards C3 and P3 at a frequency of 1-2 Hz rhythmically and monomorphically consistent with periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLED's).  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-40 mV seen continuously throughout the recording.  At times there is an overriding beta that occurs infrequently and also some diffuse burst suppression for less than 1 to 2 seconds. 
Seizures:
On 10/21/2012 at 9:43am and on 10/22/2012 at 4:01am there were seizures seen on the EEG with a sudden increase in frequency to a beta discharge of about 13-14 Hz maximally over the left frontal F3 electrode and C3 central electrode which continues to increase in frequency and in about a minute there appears to be an electrographic correlate, a clinical correlate, with right facial twitching.  This discharge continues for about 1-2 minutes and then suddenly stops and subsides.  It remains mainly over the left hemisphere and does not appear to spread to the right.  
On 10/22/2012 at 8:24 a.m. the patient develops a similar rhythmic beta discharge which increases in frequency and appears to settle over the left frontal (F3) and moves centrally (C3) region, however, remaining only over the left hemisphere.  This seizures lasts about 1 minute and has no clinical correlate to it.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to left frontal central seizures, two with a focal motor correlate in the right face, as well as one electrographic, in addition to left frontal central PLED's and diffuse slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG revealed 3 left frontal central seizures, two with a focal motor correlate and one electrographic seizure, which is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy.  In addition, there is evidence of left frontal central acute structural dysfunction and irritability.  Lastly, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific, but maybe due at least in part to medication effect. 
These findings were reported to the primary team after review of the EEG.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 55-year-old woman transferred from an outside hospital with progressive left-sided weakness, intermittent left-sided twitching, confusion, MRSA pneumonia, multiple systemic issues, and left-sided meningioma.
		
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Vimpat, Ativan, Protonix, and others.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at the bedside in the ICU using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG.  The patient was poorly responsive.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The background EEG is markedly abnormal.  The left hemisphere is diffusely slow with a mixture of theta and delta.  There is generous beta.  The right hemisphere demonstrates high amplitude, arrhythmic delta activity, with periodic complexes in the right hemisphere with some variability but typically central temporal, sometimes parietal.  This activity waxes and wanes.  It has a characteristics of a PLEDS plus. 
Although there are no clinical features, this activity seems to evolve at 1:41 waxing and waning as the patient seems to drift off to sleep.  As it terminates there is a brief period of background suppression for about 1.5 seconds as it seems to be ending. 
Heart rate:  90 bpm.
IMPRESSIONS:  This is an abnormal EEG due to:
Continuous periodic complexes in the right hemisphere with variability and waxing and waning.
At least 1 electrographic seizure in this routine bedside recording.
Marked focal slowing on the right.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  Based on these findings, continuous EEG monitoring was initiated.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 22-year-old female with a history of seizures after a stereotactic frontal craniotomy for a central neurocytoma and VP shunt, now with recurrent seizures, low Dilantin levels.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin 1 gram, Ativan 12 mg, as well as added Ativan afterward; later intubated and placed on propofol.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG for 18 hours was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional EKG and anterior temporal electrodes.  The patient was comatose and unresponsive during the EEG.  No activating procedures were performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffuse background which is disorganized, mainly in the delta/theta frequencies with no normal posterior dominant rhythm and no normal frontocentral beta.  No normal sleep architecture is also seen.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges seen at T4-T6 at a frequency of 2 Hz with amplitude of 20 to 75 microvolts.  These discharges are seen continuously but appear to evolve as described in the seizure paragraph.  During the recording, these PLEDs become more blunted and less frequent, but they are continuously seen.  At times during the last part of the recording, they appeared to have more anterior predominance, moving from T4 forward to the F8 region.
Diffuse slow waves seen in the recording. 
SEIZURES:   Frequent seizures are seen emanating from the T6-T4 electrode which evolve from PLEDs and stop.  There is a contralateral left hemisphere artifact as well as in the video left arm trembling can be seen during these seizures.  There are multiple of these occurring at the beginning of the recording, almost continuously. However, as the over time the recording improves, the periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges become more blunted and the seizures stop.  During the rest of the EEG, the last 10 hours of the EEG, there is only intermittent muscle artifact on the left hemisphere but no clear actual seizures being seen.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Frequent seizures arising from the left posterotemporal region, which later improve, and none seen during the last 10 hours.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization epilepsy arising from the right posterotemporal region, with multiple seizures seen from that region.  Please note that the seizures improve and none are seen during the last 10 hours. In addition there is continuous evidence of acute focal dysfunction and irritability in the right hemisphere.  Lastly, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction which in this patient is likely due, at least in part, to medication affect.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 25-year-old man with a history of mental retardation, cerebral palsy, spastic quadriplegia, ventilator-dependent respiratory failure, seizure disorder, hypertension, dilated cardiomyopathy, hydrocephalus, and a history of encephalitis, now with recurrent seizures, rule out status.
MEDICATIONS:  Narcotics, zonisamide, primidone, benzodiazepines.  Will add the rest later.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with an anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while lethargic.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed.  Overall the background is of low amplitude consistent with delta slowing at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of less than 20 microvolts seen continuously.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen frequently at C44/F4 seen almost continuously throughout the recording.  
Focal slow waves seen continuously at C44/F4 at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and an amplitude of 20-70 microvolts with additional asymmetric increased amplitude and faster frequencies consistent with a breach.
SEIZURES:  Throughout the recording, there were about 6 seizures which appeared to have an electrographic onset, sometimes at O2 and at times at C4 beginning with high-frequency beta discharge that could again start on the occipital region or the central region and increase in frequency and then in amplitude, later to slow down in frequency but increase in amplitude and would spread either from the occipital region towards the central region at C4 or from the central region to the occipital region and continue to evolve.  They would last for about 1 minute to 2 minutes.  Clinically, the patient appears to open his eyes and roll his eyes towards the right and may have some twitching of his arm and mouth movements.  The tech did question him once and he remained unresponsive.
HEART RATE:  120s between seizures and 134 while having a seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to recurrent seizures as well as right central focal slowing and sharp waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of status epilepticus with multiple seizures arising either from the right occipital or central region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral, structural cerebral dysfunction and irritability over the right central and frontal region.  These findings were discussed with the primary team as soon as the EEG was read.  Long-term EEG will be performed.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  Reason for study:  Seizure.
MEDICATIONS:  Zonisamide, primidone, benzodiazepines, narcotics.
INTRODUCTION:  A long term EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode. The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens with slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture and no activating procedures were performed.  Overall, the background consists of low amplitude delta frequency slowing occurring continuously throughout the recording. 
Abnormal discharges:
Focal sharp waves seen almost continuously over the C4 and CZ while in wakefulness and sleep.  There is also some O2 sharp waves seen intermittently.  
Focal slowing at C4 CZ at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and 20-60 microvolts seen continuously when in increased amplitude compared to the left consistent with a bridged rhythm.  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz amplitude of 20-30 microvolts seen continuously throughout the recording. 
Seizures:  The patient continued to have frequent seizures that appear to arise mainly at CZ4/C4 with crisp beta discharge that appears to increase in amplitude and slow down in frequency, mainly remaining over the right central frontal region.  At times there appears to be spread to the right occipital region and at times it appears to frankly originate from the right occipital region at O2.  Clinically, the patient appears to have mouth movements and eye movements.  The seizures were very frequent at the beginning of the recording and appeared to slow down towards the last hour of the recording.  At the beginning there appeared to be 2-3 seizures per hour and towards the end there appeared to be 1 seizure per hour seen in the recording.   
Heart rate:  144.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to multiple recurrent frequent seizures arising from the right central region and/or right occipital region, in addition to focal slowing and sharp waves from that region.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of status epilepticus with seizures arising from the right central region, in addition to the right occipital region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal structural dysfunction in areas that leaded from that same region.  These findings were reported to the primary team as they occurred.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  78 year old woman status post PEA code for 40 minutes, then 6 minutes in the emergency room, now with episodes of diffuse body shaking.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote, Atropine, Lasix, Lipitor, Hydralazine, Pantoprazole
REASON FOR STUDY:  Myoclonus.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode.  The patient was unresponsive during the recording.  No activating procedures were performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Some sweat artifact and movement artifact.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record has a diffusely slow background in between the bursts that I will describe in the next paragraph.  She has a markedly suppressed background with no clear electrocerebral activity.  However, this is in between much artifact which makes it difficult to make any clear final determinations about electrical activity.  In addition, she has bursts of generalized periodic epileptiform discharges with frontal predominant sharp waves and polyspikes that appear to at times increase in rhythmicity to a frequency of about 4-5 Hz and last for about 30-40 seconds.  During these bursts, which are usually symmetric, but at times slightly asymmetric, the patient appears to be clinically shaking and twitching and jerking.  These are consistent with myoclonic jerks.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  As above.
SEIZURES:  Multiple myoclonic seizures as described above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Frequent myoclonic seizures of prolonged duration in between markedly abnormal suppressed background.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of diffuse cortical injury and irritability with multiple bursts of generalized periodic discharges and associated jerks consistent with myoclonic seizures.  The interictal pattern as well as the presence of myoclonus in the setting of hypoxic injury suggests a very poor prognosis.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  55 year old female who presented to ER with sore throat felt to have epiglottitis, multiple intubation attempts failed, ended up having a PEA code with 3 rounds of epinephrine, pulse finally returned.  Circulation now about 4-6 hours later noted to have convulsive seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Ativan, Versed drip, Dilantin.
REASON FOR EEG:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Long term digital video EEG was performed for 14 hours using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with a 1 channel EKG and anterior temporal electrodes.  The study was recorded while the patient was comatose.  No activity procedures were performed.  Continuous EEG monitoring was performed and reviewed using spike detection and seizure detection software as well as nursing noted events.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a low amplitude diffusely slow background with frequencies mainly in the delta range.  No normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta is seen.  No sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:
Generalized periodic epileptiform discharges (GPEDs) were seen with anterior central maximum at a frequency of 1-2 Hz.  At times this would evolve, see description of seizures below.
Generalized slow waves seen at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and an amplitude of 20-40 microvolts.   
SEIZURES:  There are multiple seizures occurring per hour seen at the beginning of the recording which originate from GPEDs which increase in frequency and amplitude in seconds and later are obscured by a rhythmic intermittent muscle artifact.  The longest of these last about 40 seconds and afterwards  the background returns to baseline.  These seizures appear to subside 4 hours into the recording and after 10:30 at night no more are seen.  After this time the GPEDs appear to become slightly blunted and lower in amplitude, although at times intermittently the morphology changes to more sharply contoured but never clearly evolve into seizures.  Later in the recording in the morning hours there is some intermittent diffuse muscle artifact but no underlying evolution of seizures or epileptiform activity could be clearly seen in between the bursts of muscle.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:  
Generalized seizures.  
Diffuse slow waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals multiple seizures with a generalized onset and interictal evidence of diffuse brain dysfunction and irritability.  These findings are consistent with a diffuse cortical injury and with a history of hypoxic injury.  The EEG appears to improve with time and during the last 10 hours of the recording no seizures can be clearly seen.

CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 53-year-old male with longstanding refractory epilepsy, now with crescendo seizures, seizure-related fall, and complaint of left-sided numbness.  
MEDICATIONS: Dilantin and Depakote. 
INTRODUCTION: Continuous video EEG monitoring is performed in the unit using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: The patient reports no seizures during this portion of the record. Reviewing the data, no clinical or electrographic seizures occurred.  
RANDOM WAKEFULNESS AND SLEEP: In wakefulness, the background EEG is well organized.  In the overnight sleep record, there are occasional left temporal spike and slow wave complexes.  No generalized epileptiform discharges are seen in the sleep record.  
HR:  80 BPM.
	IMPRESSION/CLINICAL CORRELATION: This EEG monitoring spanned 10/11-15/06.  A single seizure was recorded on the first day of admission.  No additional seizures were recorded and unfortunately the EEG recording from the 14th through to the date of discharge on the 15th is not available.  
	
	Abnormalities included a single seizure, which began as a complex partial seizure and was not well localized by scalp EEG.  There were interictal epileptiform discharges.  In the past for this patient there have been right temporal findings and generalized spike and wave activity, but in the last piece of EEG provided, there are left temporal sharp waves suggesting a more widespread epileptogenic foci than previously recognized.  


CLINICAL HISTORY:  53 year old right handed woman with a history of seizures and alcohol abuse, now with intermittent episodes of confusion and agitation, concerning for seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat 50 mg b.i.d.
CONDITIONS OF RECORDING:  Digital video continuous EEG performed was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is a right to left interhemispheric asymmetry.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the left hemisphere consists of a low to moderate amplitude 9 to 10 Hz alpha activity with preserved anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the right hemisphere consists of 79 Hz activity with weak anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  During sleep, sleep spindles and at least epoch of REM sleep is captured.  There is additional continuous polymorphic theta and delta activity over the right hemisphere, most notably over the right temporal region.  Independent right anterior temporal frontocentral epileptiform discharges and additional independent right midtemporal as well as independent right posterior quadrant/occipital epileptiform discharges are captured.  Phase reversal occurs over F8, T6, and O2 electrodes, respectively.  As the recording progresses, frequency of right occipital/right posterior quadrant epileptiform discharges increases.  A single episode concerning for an electrographic seizure is captured.  It starts at about 10:45 p.m., lasting until about 10:50 p.m.  It is characterized by rhythmic slow delta activity observed over the right hemisphere, most notably over the right temple region with embedded sharp waves over T6 and O2 electrodes.  The patient appears to wake up at the beginning of the seizure.  Also, there is an increase in the heart rate observed in a single EKG lead from about 120 beats a minute to about 130 to 140 beats a minute.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation are not performed.
FINDINGS:  
Focal slowing over the right hemisphere, most notably over the right temple region.
Independent epileptiform discharges over the right anterior temporal central region, right midtemporal region, and right posterior quadrant/occipital region.  The frequency of these epileptiform discharges increases as the recording progresses.
Likely a single electrographic right hemispheric, primarily right midtemporal/right posterior quadrant electrographic seizure is captured at approximately 10:45 p.m.  This seizure lasted approximately 5 minutes.  There is an associated increase in the heart rate during the event, as well as the patient awakening at the beginning of the event.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal continuous, about 15 hour, video EEG due to:
Continuous moderate right hemispheric slowing, most notable over the right midtemporal region.
Independent right anterior frontocentral, right midtemporal, and right posterior quadrant/occipital region epileptiform discharges.
Likely a single approximately 5-minute focal right hemispheric, primarily right midtemporal/right posterior quadrant electrographic seizure.   
CLINICAL CORRELATION:   These findings indicate a significant dysfunction over the right hemisphere, likely structural in etiology with increased epileptogenic potential.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology consulting team at the time of the review.


REASON FOR STUDY:  Unresponsiveness status post code.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 50-year-old woman with known thyroid cancer discovered 15 years ago with progressive dysphagia who had emergency surgery remove mass.  She became acutely bradycardic.  She had CPR started and coded for about 12 minutes.  She had a change in mental status after the code.
MEDICATIONS:  Zofran, albuterol, insulin, Ancef, sodium chloride, chlorhexidine, __________, magnesium oxide, amiodarone, Tylenol, Protonix, vitamin K, Zosyn, vancomycin.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with the addition of anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  There was a 60 Hz artifact seen in the F1 and F2 electrodes throughout the recording.
DESCRIPTION OF RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There was no normal sleep architectural and no activating procedures were performed.  Overall, the background was consistent with a diffuse delta pattern at 2-4 Hz and an amplitude 20-40 microvolts.  __________ there is frontal by frontal slowing and sharp waves seen frequently throughout the recording.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Bifrontal and sometimes central sharp waves seen at CZ, F3 and F4, and at times at FP1 or FP2.  
Focal slowing in the bifrontal region. 
Generalized slowing as described before.
SEIZURES:  At 1:21 p.m. the EEG recording suddenly stopped and the recording restarts at 1:24 p.m.  At this point there is a clear prefrontal rhythmic discharge consistent of sharp waves maximal mainly at FP1, but at times at FP2 at a frequency of 3-4 Hz going on continuously with no clear clinical correlate.  This pattern is consistent with an electrographic seizure.  The electrographic seizure continues without stopping until about almost 19 minutes later after receiving Ativan at 1:39 p.m.  After that the EEG appears to go back to a diffusely slow background with some prefrontal slowing.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to an electrographic prefrontal maximal seizure with no clear onset seen as well as bifrontal slowing and generalized slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals electrographic seizures which appears to be maximally in the frontal region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the frontal region as well as diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regards to etiology, but is likely related to the hypoxic injury.  These findings were discussed with the team and the patient will be placed on long-term monitoring.

DURATION OF STUDY:  Study date 03/26/2013 through 03/27/2013, for a total of 15 hours.  However, due to technical difficulties only 5 hours could be reviewed.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  39-year-old woman with episode of bilateral arm stiffness and gaze deviated to the right.  Has 4-5 seizures in the past month, screaming, with a history of Lennox-Gastaut, mental retardation.  Follow-up EEG.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Ativan, famotidine, Lovenox, topiramate, Flagyl, Depakote.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement with the addition of anterior temporal single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software in addition to nurse's notes were used to review the EEG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm and frontocentral beta.  Overall, there are theta delta mixed frequencies intermingled with multiple multifocal spikes which I will describe in Abnormal Discharges.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Multifocal spikes seen at O1, O2, T6, Fp1, Fp2, T3, and P4.  However, the spikes are mainly seen at O1 and O2 and Fp1/Fp2.  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts seen almost continuously throughout the recording.
SEIZURES:  During the record, there are multiple seizures which in the EEG appear to begin suddenly and display rhythmic, sharply contoured theta intermixed with beta that at times appears to be maximal frontally and then centrally with an overlying rhythmic occipital sharp focus.  The maximal focus or origin of the seizure is unclear.  It is also atypical as it does not appear to evolve as it suddenly starts, remains in the EEG at almost this irregular but continuous frequency, and then appears to slowly dissipate.  During these discharges, the patient appears to move her head side to side and sometimes moves her arms in a wild fashion.  On 1 occasion, her mouth appeared to twitch in the video.  The duration of these episodes can be anywhere from 1 or 2 minutes to prolonged 38-minute seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to multiple seizures with unclear origin and multifocal spikes.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of multiple generalized seizures with unclear origin as well as multiple areas of irritability and structural dysfunction as well as diffuse dysfunction consistent with diagnosis of symptomatic generalized epilepsy.   
These findings were communicated to the primary team as soon as the EEG was read.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 39-year-old woman with mental retardation and Lennox-Gastaut with recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Ativan, famotidine, Lovenox, topiramate, Flagyl, Depakote, and Vimpat.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike detection as well as seizure detection software was used to review the events.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  The EEG's multiple electrical artifact improved during the day, however, there was still some movement artifact seen throughout the recording obscuring some of the seizures.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta.  Overall there are theta-delta mixed frequencies intermingled with multiple multifocal spikes, which I will describe in the abnormal discharges.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Multifocal spikes seen at O1, O2, T6, FP1, FP2, T3, and T4.  However, the majority of the spikes are seen on O1, O2 and less so at FP1/FP2.  
Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz at an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts seen almost continuously throughout the recording.
SEIZURES:  Again during the recording there are multiple seizures, about 13 of them, some of them lasting up to 30 minutes where the EEG appears to seldom display a rhythmic sharply contoured theta intermixed at beta and at times maximally frontally and centrally with an overlying rhythmic occipital sharp focus.  It is unclear where the origin of the seizure is.  It is also atypical in that it does not clearly evolve, it just suddenly starts and appears to slowly stop.  The patient appears to move her head side to side and arms moving in a wild flinging fashion.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to multiple seizures with unclear origin and multifocal spikes.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of multiple generalized seizure of unclear origin as well as multiple areas of irritability and structural dysfunction as well as diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is consistent with the diagnosis of symptomatic generalized epilepsy.  These findings were conveyed to the primary team.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  26 year old right handed female with a history of tuberous sclerosis, Lupus nephritis, and brain lesions respected at age 7, with expressive aphasia at baseline.  Follow-up EEG for a history of seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Versed, Ativan, Heparin, Compazine, Vimpat.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Change in mental status.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video  EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and EKG electrodes.  No activating procedures were performed.  The patient was lethargic and unresponsive during the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow EEG with no posterior dominant rhythm and no clear frontocentral beta.  Some eye movements can be seen.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:

Generalized slow waves at 2-4 Hz

IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Rhythmic right posterior quadrant PLEDs.  
Generalized slow waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization epilepsy with seizures possibly arising from the right posterior quadrant.  There is also
