CLINICAL HISTORY:  20 year old with Noonan's syndrome, AICD, mental retardation, macrocephaly, admitted with low medication levels and recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Ativan, Versed, Phenobarbital, Vimpat, Dilantin.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG. The patient has just had a prolonged generalized seizure and is quite somnolent.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The EEG demonstrates a slow background with generous beta and arrhythmic delta.  High amplitude spike and wave and polyspike and wave activity can be identified independently from the right or left hemisphere.  On the right it tends to be maximum in the central temporal region with generous spread to the posterior regions.  Vertex waves and spindles are noted as the tracing continues and the patient's postictal period begins to resolve.  Stimulation of the patient produces an arousal.   
HR:   120 bpm
IMPRESSION:  This postictal record is remarkable for:
Prominent beta activity.
Prominent background slowing.
High amplitude epileptiform activity with spike and wave and polyspike and wave activity, which is most prominent on the right.
LONG TERM MONITORING - # 12-30 – 03/11/2012
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  In the overnight recording, the patient is asleep.  There are intermittent sharp waves noted from the right or left.  There is a significant amount of EKG artifact.   
At 8:55, while the patient is sleeping on his back with his pillow over him, he seems to have had an electrographic seizure without clinical correlate.  This begins with a burst of fast activity from the right hemisphere of 9.5 Hz, lasting about 5 seconds before we see irregular sharp waves which are isoelectric at T2 and T4 but with a T4 phase reversal.  This demonstrates frequent evolution and the event seems to be over in about 30 seconds.  A similar 30-second discharge is noted at 9:16 in the morning, again without clinical correlate.  The patient is tested at 9:20.  He is arousable but drifting off to sleep.  The awake EEG for him in this section of the record is remarkable for a slow background with restlessness.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 24-year-old male admitted with 3 tonic-clonic seizures which began with shaking of the left arm and mouth and then went on to convulsive activity, followed by obtundation.
MEDICATIONS:  Vimpat, Keppra, benzodiazepines, others.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed in the lab using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with one channel of EKG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  As the tracing began, the patient is in ICU and is very confused and moving about.  The background EEG is markedly abnormal with primarily a theta pattern, poorly organized with generous background beta.  In addition, there are sharp waves noted in the record with more than one localization including C3 and the right temporal region.  There is prominent theta noted in the background. 
A burst of right temporal sharp waves and delta was noted at 2:35 but without a clear clinical correlate.  This burst continues as might be anticipated in an electrographic seizure but looking at the video the patient does not appear to be doing anything other than motion arrest.  There is no significant change in the patient's behavior after this 30-second discharge.  Additional bursts of right temporal sharp waves and spikes maximum in the anterior temporal region were noted at 2:47.  At the close of the EEG, the right temporal sharp waves are building up.  They have not yet evolved into the pattern of a seizure.  Of note, the patient has turned his head to the left but testing is not performed and it is difficult to determine if there is really a change in the patient's mental status.  The patient is confused but interactive throughout the EEG. 
Heart rate 90 BPM.
IMPRESSION:  This is a markedly abnormal EEG due to:  
Marked background slowing and disorganization.  
Prominent right anterior temporal sharp waves and spikes.  
At least 1 electrographic seizure from the right temporal region.  
Sharp waves noted on occasion from the left hemisphere as well.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  In this individual who has had recent, repetitive seizures, this is an ongoing ictal record although it does not meet the formal criteria for a status epilepticus record and the seizure that was recorded appeared to be with little clinical signs or symptoms and was brief.   
Additional EEG recording was initiated on the evening of the 16th.

intubated and unresponsive.

voltage, slow featureless pattern. When reviewed at lower sensitivities, low voltage 
beta is noted. There is an asymmetry in the delta and there is more delta in the right 
posterior quadrant. Twenty-four hours of EEG recording is provided. There is little 
clinical information documented in the record.

noted as well as some sharply contoured delta at T 4. Over time, there is an increase in 
frontal beta activity and intermittent arousal. By the afternoon, the patient's electrodes 
are sometimes a bit challenged but then are repaired by the technologist. Overall, over 
the course of the record, there is an overall improvement. 
In this intubated, comatose patient, what is likely an electrographic seizure is noted at 
4:52:32 p.m. Although there is some electrode artifact, there does seem to be some 
frequency evolution. Following the seizure, the patient is more alert. He is noted to be 
moving about. As the recording continues into the end of the 24-hour epoch, there are 
intermittent problems with the electrodes. Review of the automated seizure and spike 
detection software does not identify other electrographic seizures. 
HR: 120 bpm

1. Initially a markedly suppressed pattern. 
2. A single subcritical seizure observed at 4:35:22 p.m. on the afternoon of the 9th. 
3. Occasional sharply contoured right anterior temporal delta. 
Right posterior temporal delta.

mechanism for epilepsy. Additional evaluation may be obtained as warranted.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 73-year-old woman with seizure in the nursing home, lasting 1 hour, now confused with history of old left hemispheric stroke.MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Dilantin, heparin.INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using a standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode.  The patient is recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffuse slow background with no posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  Overall the background is consistent of delta frequency slowing, which is seen maximal over the right hemisphere.  The left hemisphere reveals rhythmic sharp waves and intermittent suppression, which I will discuss, and abnormal discharges.  No activating procedures were performed.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  1. Focal sharp waves seen continuously over the left hemisphere at a frequency in a periodic manner occurring at 1 Hz, maximal at F74/T3, consistent with periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLED's).  In between these PLED's there is suppression of faster frequencies over that hemisphere.  2. Focal slowing seen continuously over the right hemisphere at F84/T4, seen throughout the recording at a frequency of 2-5 Hz and amplitude of 20-50 microvolts.SEIZURES:  Throughout the recording there are frequent recurrent seizures arising from the right hemisphere, appearing to be maximal at T4, starting with a beta frequency discharge that appears to slow down in frequency, increase in amplitude and spread to the right hemisphere.  It appears to remain mainly over the right hemisphere and slowly subside afterwards, leaving some postictal focal slowing in the right anterior temporal region.  These discharges have no clinical correlate and are consistent with nonconvulsive seizures or electrographic seizures.  They are occurring frequently, multiple times throughout the recording and last from 30 second to 1 minute.HEART RATE:  108.IMPRESSION:  Abnormal electroencephalogram due to recurring right midtemporal electrographic seizure in relation to left anterior temporal periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges.CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals electrographic nonconvulsive status epilepticus arising from the right midtemporal region.  In addition, there is focal cerebral dysfunction in that region, which may signify a structural lesion or focal irritability in that region.  In addition, there is independent focal cerebral dysfunction and irritability over the left anterior temporal region, which may also signify another focus of structural dysfunction and irritability.  These findings were communicated to the primary team.LENGTH OF RECORDING:  26 minutes.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 59-year-old gentleman with a history of schizophrenia who presents with altered mental status and at least 2 episodes of tonic posturing with decreased responsiveness in the setting of ingesting Vicks VapoRub.
MEDICATIONS:  Lovenox, Ativan 1 mg at 6:00 a.m. and several mgs on 01/13/2013.
INTRODUCTION:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrode with a single EKG lead.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior-to-posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  There is right-to-left interhemispheric asymmetry with slower wave forms observed over the left hemisphere.  The background consists of a mixture of diffuse beta, theta, and delta activity.  Additional polymorphic often sharply contoured delta is observed over the left hemisphere, primarily over the left temporal region.  Several electrographic seizures are captured in this recording.  Seizures have abrupt onset without clear localization or lateralization.  Electrographically diffuse EMG artifact is observed.  Once the EMG artifact resolves generalized sharply contoured approximately 3 Hz rhythmic delta activity is observed diffusely, but it is higher in amplitude over the left hemisphere.  This activity increases in amplitude and decreases in frequency to approximately 2 Hz and often evolves into another episode of generalized EMG artifact that obscures the recording.  Once that EMG artifact resolves, rhythmic sharply contoured 3-4 Hz delta activity is often observed over the left temple region.   
Clinically it appears that the patient moans and slobbers at the onset of seizure and has decreased responsiveness during the seizure.
FINDINGS:  
Focal status epilepticus.  
Excess beta.
Left hemispheric slowing most pronounced over the left temporal region.
Mild to moderate diffuse slowing.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 26-minute electroencephalogram due to diffuse slowing, focal left hemispheric slowing that is most pronounced over the left temporal region and multiple focal left temporal electrographic seizures without returning back to baseline.  Diffuse background slowing indicates etiologically nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction.  Focal left hemispheric slowing that is most pronounced over the temporal region indicates focal cerebral dysfunction, and in this case, could partially be to related post-ictal phenomenon.  Multiple electrographic and clinical seizures captured during the recording are consistent with focal status epilepticus.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology consult team at the time of the recording.


STUDY DATE:  01/14/2013 to 01/15/2013, for a total of 18 hours.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 59-year-old man with CAD, schizophrenia, COPD, and cocaine use who presents with recurrent change in mental status, ingestion of Vick's VapoRub, and recurrent seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Ativan, phenobarbital, propofol, dopamine.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software and nurses' notes were used to review the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Some electrode artifact in the ear (A,- A2) electrodes halfway through the recording.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a background with no clear posterior dominant rhythm and mainly diffuse beta continuously seen.  There is no normal sleep architecture and no activating procedures were performed.  Overall there appears to be an overriding beta and background slowing underneath the diffuse beta.   
Abnormal discharges:  Focal slow waves seen frequently over F7/T3 at a frequency of 2 to 4 Hz and an amplitude of 20 to 40 microvolts. 
Seizures:  The patient has recurrent electrographic seizures that appear to have an unclear onset but mainly seem to develop from a beta discharge in the prefrontal region, mainly at FP1, which quickly spreads to the contralateral hemisphere and is obscured by muscle artifact.  Afterward there is diffuse delta that remains slightly more prominently over the left hemisphere until it dissipates.  The average seizure lasts about 2 minutes.  During the first recording, he was having about 5 or 6 seizures per hour.  As the time goes along, it decreases to 5, then 4 seizures per hour.  The last hour that they stopped, from 1:00 to 2:00 a.m., he has about 3 seizures.  After that there are no more seizures seen.  During the seizures, it is hard to clearly see any clear correlation in the video, but there appears to be some muscle artifact seen, noted in the EEG as described above, consistent with an EEG clinical correlate.  After 2:00 a.m., no seizures were seen.  Some burst suppression is noted, but mainly the same background remains.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to multiple recurrent seizures of unclear onset, but possibly left prefrontal region with additional left anterior temporal focal slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of status epilepticus with multiple seizures possibly arising from the left prefrontal region, which appears to have stopped by 2:00 a.m.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the left anterior temporal region, which is nonspecific with regard to etiology.  Lastly, the diffuse beta may be due, at least in part, to medication effect.  There is underlying generalized slowing which is nonspecific with regard to etiology.  Please note that the findings on this EEG were communicated to the primary team throughout the night at intervals.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  61 year old gentleman with stroke, left MCA infarct, recurrent status epilepticus, cardiac arrest due January 22, 2012 for 9 minutes, followed by right gaze deviation with left hand shaking.  Evaluate for recurrent seizures.  Persistent obtundation.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Lamictal, Dopamine, Dobutamine, Keppra.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement.  The patient is poorly responsive and has a tracheostomy.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The background EEG is markedly abnormal.  Multiple electrographic seizures are noted with high amplitude epileptiform activity from the left hemisphere, typically maximum in the left frontal central region, but with a generous field of spread.  
The spikes seems to be maximum at C3, but fast activity is seen throughout the left frontal central region with more periodic activity left frontal.  In some locations there seems to be a posterior to anterior lag or even more than one epileptiform focus in the left temporal region, but the ictal driver seems to be in the left frontal central region.  
Seizures are typically on the duration of 1-2 minutes.  Characteristic frequency of evolution of the seizures was observed.   
Despite the marked background slowing, the patient seems to be somewhat responsive.  During the seizures, he has head turning to the right, but not really a forced deviation, more of a more gentle head turning.  
When he is not seizing, he does seem to demonstrate some head control and some spontaneous movement on the left when the seizures stop. 
The background EEG is otherwise markedly slow bilaterally.   
HR:    120 bpm
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:
A status epilepticus pattern with multiple, repetitive seizures from the left hemisphere.
Sharp waves and spikes in the left.
Intermittent burst suppressed pattern when the patient is not seizing. 
Some generalized periodic complexes observed in the interictal section of the record.
Some posterior predominant generalized periodic discharges also noted in the interictal period.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This patient has previously been evaluated for status epilepticus out of the left hemisphere.  This EEG may demonstrate some features which are due to anoxic encephalopathy, as well as the ictal pattern.

CLINICAL HISTORY: 55 year old male with epilepsy , diabetes, diverticulitis, fibromyalgia, childhood closed head injury. 
MEDICATIONS: Ativan, Keppra, Lamictal, Phenobarbital, Haldol 
INTRODUCTION: Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. In the initial sections of the record, The patient is started on Phenobarbital and has an Ativan drip. The Phenobarbital is started on 11:26 a.m. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: The background EEG includes a pattern with generous beta. The patient is somnolent in the early hours of the EEG and there are bursts of paroxysmal higher amplitude slowing. 
The patient is quite restless through the EEG. He has difficulty cooperating with the EEG monitoring and the electrodes are essentially uninterruptable on the morning of the 7th. 
There was a seizure reported at 3:30 in the morning. The patient remains on video camera at 3:30 in the morning. The video is reviewed. There is no evidence of a behavioral or electrographic seizure that can be identified on the video at 3:30 in the morning. 
The subsequent EEG recording on the 7th demonstrates an increase in the awake individual with a well defined alpha rhythm, generous beta. No intraictal epileptiform features are observed. Again, a well defined awake pattern is noted at 1:23 in the afternoon. 
Seizure time 4:00 p.m. 
A prolonged behavioral event is noted at 3:55p.m. with the patient rocking to the right and head turning to the right. This produces head rocking artifact in an otherwise awake individual. Tactile stimulation of the patient does not change the behavior and the patient looks away from the stimulus. 
The event is relatively long and at 4:00 p.m. the event stops abruptly and there are no postictal changes noted. Ativan was given which produced drowsiness. 
The patient is described as stiff at 4:07. The EEG demonstrates a normal awake pattern. Another episode of head shaking is noted at 4:35 again, without a change in the EEG. Ongoing recording demonstrates an awake and drowsy pattern. 
The behaviors recorded are most supportive of a nonepileptic process. 
HR: 84 bpm
IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to: 
1. Excess beta. 
INTRODUCTION: Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG. The patient is extremely agitated, and he receives phenobarbital, Ativan, and Haldol. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: In wakefulness, there is generous beta. Motor activity and behaviors were associated with an increase in muscle artifact, but no epileptiform activity. 
The patient is able to keep the EEG electrodes on, but they start to come off at 2:20. 
HR: 90 bpm 
IMPRESSION: This EEG monitoring session was remarkable for: 
1. Multiple behavior events without EEG correlate. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: The excess beta may be associated with this patient's medications. No epileptiform features were noted. 


CLINICAL HISTORY:   69 year old woman with epilepsy, admitted with small bowel obstruction, made n.p.o. with recurrent seizures starting on the 20th.
MEDICATIONS:  Midazolam, Ativan, phenobarbital.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG.  The patient is having clinical and subclinical seizures in the initial section of the record.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  As the tracing begins, recurrent seizures typically lasting about a minute long are identified.  These are maximum at T1, FP1, and F3.  They demonstrate characteristic frequency of elution but not all are seceded with the patient's clinical event, which is clenching of both arms upward.  There is clear evolution and there is a clear pattern of a discontinuous pattern when the seizures terminate.  The background from the right hemisphere is a mixture of beta and delta.   
The patient has a low blood pressure and requires a central line and pressure support prior to the infusion of medications.  At 8:30 and at 10:00 p.m. she received 300 mg of phenobarbital, with additional 100 mg at 7:01 a.m.  This produces bradycardia.  Heart rate 48 bpm at 12:46 a.m.  The EEG does start to break up with this dose, but then a burst of sharp waves is noted at 12:47, and then a seizure 2 minutes later.   
Midazolam is initiated and titrated upward.  Epileptiform activity is seen at 5:56 a.m.  Versed reaches its maximum dose at 7:40:59.  By 7:43, the epileptiform activity is not as prominent.  There are bursts of sharp waves or fast activity from the left frontal or frontocentral region.  There are no additional clinical seizures noted.  Although there are bursts, some of which are quite prominent, none of them have the completely characteristic frequency of elution noted in the initial sections of the record.  The patient gradually receives additional doses of medications.   
The patient is maintained on Midazolam with small doses of phenobarbital.  Although there are electrographic seizures noted between 9:00 and 10:00 a.m., by 19:00 hours the epileptiform activity is waning without the same discrete pattern of clearly defined seizures but more of a prominent pattern of bursts of epileptiform activity.   
HR:    48 bpm
LONG TERM MONITORING - # 11-113 – 11/21/2011 TO 11/22/2011
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. 
No clinical seizures were recorded.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  This section of the record is remarkable for intermittent bursts of sharply contoured activity in the left frontal and frontotemporal/centrotemporal region.  This typically occurs with a burst of delta, then fast activity and then underlying spike.  Most bursts are less than 10 seconds in duration.  There are sections of the record with bursts lasting up to 20 seconds.  There is also intermittent discontinuity.  At 7:00 p.m. is a section of the record with a bit more in the way of epileptiform activity.   
At 11:22 there is an increase in muscle noted in the background.  Rather movements of the patient's upper extremities are noted.  There are certainly sections where she seems to be coughing on the vent.  This section of the record does include prominent epileptiform activity, but no definitive seizures, particularly on the morning of November 22.   
By 2:11 in the afternoon, the EEG is better and the patient is actually moving a bit.  At that point the electrodes are discontinued briefly. 
HR:    78 bpm
LONG TERM MONITORING - # 11-113 – 11/20/2011 to 11/21/2011
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG.  Continuous seizure and spike detection is utilized.  There is no clinical information provided in the record and there is no medication up date.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The background EEG demonstrates a continuous pattern with generous beta particularly in the frontal regions.  There are sections with a discontinuous pattern, particularly at 7:00 p.m.  There are bursts of sharply contoured activity in the left frontal region or sharp waves, and some of these occur even out of the burst suppression pattern.  It appears that pharmacotherapy might have been changed at 7:00 p.m. on the night of November 22, because there is intermittently a burst suppression pattern and an increase in epileptiform activity.   
By 2:17 a.m. the background EEG is more continuous and it is more of a theta pattern.  There are also sections of the record, including at 2:00 a.m. where the camera is moved to the door and the EEG electrodes on the left are compromised.   
This section ends at 8:03 a.m. and at this point the patient is not seizing, but there is a prominent focus with left frontal sharp waves.  
HR:   N/A
LONG TERM MONITORING - # 11-113 – 11/22/2011 to 11/23/2011
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video EEG monitoring was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.  The patient is intubated and poorly responsive.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The background EEG demonstrates a slow pattern.  There is a prominent, left frontal spike noted.  In comparison to previous sections of the record, there is significantly less in the way of bursts or buildup of the spike.  The patient is intubated and little to no motor activity is noted.  Tactile stimulation of the patient produces an increase in muscle and it does seem to produce an alerting response and perhaps even a decrease in epileptiform activity, but afterwards at 11:13 there is an increase in epileptiform activity.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  54 year old right handed female with history of seizures since childhood that was recently described as left side of the body burning lasting for a second.
MEDICATIONS:  Tegretol
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed in lab using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with an additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness.  Activating procedures, including hyperventilation and photic stimulation, were performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well defined posterior dominant rhythm of 9 Hz only seen over the left hemisphere.  Over the right hemisphere there is attenuation of all frequencies, including beta frequency, which can only be seen over the left hemisphere.  The patient is only recorded during wakefulness.   
Activating procedures produce no abnormal discharges.  However, during hyperventilation the patient does start complaining of left arm numbness and pain.  During this time and intermittently there appears to be a change in the EKG lead to what appears to be bigeminy for seconds intermittently.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES: 
Focal attenuation of the entire right hemisphere and disruption of the posterior dominant rhythm seen throughout the recording.
Mild excessive theta in wakefulness, along with bursts of FIRDA in wakefulness.   
SEIZURES:  During the recording the patient complained of her left arm hurting, which is consistent with her typical events, but there is no EEG correlate during this event.  The patient did not loose consciousness.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Right hemisphere attenuation and disruption of the posterior dominant rhythm in that hemisphere. 
Mild generalized slowing. 
FIRDA.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of focal cortical dysfunction in the right hemisphere, which is consistent with a known hemiatrophy seen on the right hemisphere for this patient.  In addition, there is evidence of mild diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regards to etiology.  The episodes of left arm burning did not have any EEG correlate.  However, please note that up to 75% of simple partial seizures may not be captured during scalp EEG.  Lastly, the patient did have some EKG abnormalities seen throughout the recording.  Due to this the patient was recommended to be seen in the emergency room right after the EEG.  She was taken there for this.

REASON FOR STUDY: Seizures 
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 61-year-old man with subdural on the right and craniotomy with EEG due to left-sided twitching.
MEDICATIONS:  At the beginning, the patient was on Dilantin and phenobarbital as well as propofol, and during the last day propofol was stopped and Versed was added.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed for 60 hours using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.  Continuous spike wave detection as well as seizure detection software and nurses' notes, patient events, as well as primary team's observations were used to review the EEG.  Information was given back to the primary team as it was reviewed multiple times during the day.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record reveals a posterior dominant rhythm which is an absent posterior dominant rhythm or normal frontocentral beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture and no activating procedures were performed.  Overall, the background is diffusely attenuated and lacks normal cerebral activity.  Intermixed with this diffusely attenuated and suppressed background, there will be bursts of sharply contoured theta occurring over only the right hemisphere with intermixed sharp waves and spikes that will be described in the "abnormal discharges," that would last anywhere from 5 to 20 seconds.  There appears to be no reactivity to the EEG; however, during the first 10 hours there appears to be some muscle activity seen in the EEG which later was not noticed with the rest of the recording.   
Abnormal discharges:
Focal sharp waves seen over the right hemisphere, maximal at T4, C4, and F8, seen frequently in the bursts.  
Severe generalized slowing. 
Seizures:  Throughout the entire recording, during the bursts of spike activity, clinically the primary team reported that they noticed intermittent facial twitching with each of the bursts.  These would be irregular and intermittent, at times getting worse and sometimes improving; however, there was not a clear EMG correlate on every single one of these twitches on the EEG.  There appears to be some possible electrical correlate, but it is not clear, throughout the recording.  Based on these clinical findings, the primary team would make decisions to continue sedation and antiepileptic drugs.  This occurred almost continuously throughout the recording.  On 08/11/2013, at around 4:40 p.m., facial twitches continued to be noted, and around 5:22 p.m., propofol was stopped and a Versed drip was started, which was later slowly increased up to 14 mg per hour.  During this time, the EEG remained similar with intermittent bursts, but they appeared to be slightly more attenuated and less frequent; however, still occurring.  Around 8:30 the bursts became extremely infrequent, and by 9:20 the bursts appeared to have stopped and disappeared.  The primary team was made aware of these changes, and at that time unfortunately the patient coded and there were multiple artifacts throughout the recording.  After this time, there was no clear electrical activity seen on the EEG, and the EEG was discontinued around 12:00 midnight. 
Heart rate 72. Please note that the heart rate became extremely bradycardic and disappeared for a period of time while the patient was coded.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to status epilepticus with seizures arising from the right hemisphere and marked generalized cerebral dysfunction. At the end of the record no electrical activity was seen.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of status epilepticus with multiple seizures arising through the right hemisphere.  In addition, there is evidence of marked generalized severe cerebral dysfunction, which may be, at least in part, due to medication effect. During the last part of the record, no normal electro-cerebral activity were seen. The findings seen and reported throughout this entire recording were communicated to the primary team as they occurred with multiple reviews of the EEG over the 60 hours.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  22 year old right handed female with a history of seizures with increased number of seizures recently, refractory to multiple medications, also with a history of nonepileptic spells.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Lamotrigine, Zonisamide.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizure classification.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term monitoring EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and EKG electrodes.  Video recording was also performed.  Continuous spike detection software as well as seizure detection software along with the patient's alarms and nurses' notes were used to review the EEG. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well-defined posterior dominant rhythm of 9 to 10 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  The patient has a normal amount of frontocentral beta.  No activating procedures were performed.  The patient was recorded in stage I and stage II and deep slow-wave sleep.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Only during sleep, some focal sharp waves were seen with a large field at FB2/F8 with small bursts seen rarely. 
DAY 1:  Two events occurred during the first day of recording.  One occurred at 1:48 p.m. and the other one occurred at 11:07 p.m.  During both of them, the patient suddenly stated that she did not feel well and suddenly started having right arm shaking and moving side to side with her eyes closed with irregular body motions and hand shaking.  During the first event, she would also lift her legs and move side to side while unresponsive.  This lasted about 2 to 3 minutes, then stopped and the patient was back to herself.  The second one was of similar clinical presentation; however, the flapping and shifting side to side was much more aggressive and severe.  This lasted about 3 minutes.  During both events, the EEG appeared to remain normal with no epileptiform activity or EEG correlate to these events. 
LONG TERM MONITORING – 12-57 – 05/09/2012 TO 05/10/2012
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  Day 2:  The record opens to a well defined posterior dominant rhythm which reaches 9 Hz with a normal frontal central beta.  This patient is recorded in stage I, stage II, stage III, and REM sleep.  No activating procedures were performed.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  During sleep, specifically stage II to III, the patient appears to have some frequent bifrontal sharp waves at FP2, as well as some more temporal sharp waves.  There is also bursts of slowing with sharply contoured activity seen with multifocal regions more frontal more temporal, but not clear if it is epileptic.   
No events were captured during this 24-hour recording.
SEIZURES:  None
This is long-term, day #3, 05/09/2012 to 05/10/2012, a total of 24 hour
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  Day #3.   
Abnormal discharges:  As stated above, focal sharp waves seen intermittently in sleep with abrupt at times more prominent in the right frontal region at FP-2 and sometimes F4, and quickly moving toward the right temporal lobe.  At times there are sharp waves that appear to be left frontal, FP3, and sometimes they appear to be more at T6 P3.  These are at times all seen in complexes that last for 1 to 2 seconds, usually mainly in sleep.   
SEIZURES:  
Complex partial seizure #1.  occurred on 05/09/2012 at 12:31 p.m. and ended at 12:34 p.m., with a total duration of 2 minutes and 14 seconds.  Description of seizure:  The patient is laying on her left side while asleep.  Suddenly she arouses, opens her eyes, and jolts her body, then her right arm moves up and becomes tonic while her left hand becomes dystonic in a spooning fashion.  The right arm then continues to lower down and stretches forward along with the left arm while her eyes go back and her moans.  She then starts having clonic jerks of both arms and body.  The lower extremities cannot be visualized clearly in the video.  She continues to have clonic jerks while moaning and then slowly the jerks start decreasing in frequency and then they stop with the last ictal jerk on the left hand.  Afterward, she lays in bed unresponsive.  She is not tested during the entire seizure.   
EEG during seizure #1:  At onset, there is a generalized attenuation of faster frequencies followed by a generalized polyspike discharge that appears to be slightly more predominant over the right hemisphere than the left with no clear area of maximality, which in about 20 seconds appears to settle on the right temporal lobe more than the left and continues to evolve in frequency and then spread to the frontal region of the right hemisphere and then seen later spreading through the frontal lobe of the left hemisphere and then generalizing and obscured by muscle artifact.  At the end of the seizure there appears to be diffuse attenuation.   
Tonic-clonic seizure #2 occurred at 4:00 in the morning and ended at 4:02 in the morning.  Seizure description:  The patient again is asleep, this time laying on her right side.  The patient opens her eyes about 8 seconds into the electrographic seizure but does not move until about 30 seconds after the electrographic seizure begins.  Suddenly both hands become dystonic and stiff while stretching forward slightly, and she remains tonic with tonic stress posturing for about 20 to 30 seconds.  Afterward she starts having tonic-clonic movements of both arms while moaning repeatedly.  She continues to do this until the jerks, after increasing frequency, start decreasing in frequency and then later stop.   
EEG description:  Again, at the onset of the seizure, there appears to be a slight diffuse attenuation and slowing with no clear onset but appears to have a slight right-sided predominance, although it is mainly generalized with polyspikes that appear to be somewhat temporal and frontal maximal.  As the seizure evolves in frequency, it appears to settle slightly over the right temporal lobe, then spreading quickly to the prefrontal region bilaterally and then is obscured by muscle artifact.  At the end of the seizure, there is diffuse attenuation seen.   
SEIZURES #3:  Occurred at 06:13 in the morning and ended at 6:15 in the morning.  Seizure description:  The patient is again asleep and opens her eyes about 8 seconds into the seizure, but lays there still and about 20 seconds later her eyes rolled up and her both arms lift up in the air in a dystonic fashion, the right slightly more than the left, followed by stretching forward and both hands becoming dystonic, and then clonic movements increasing in frequency and then decreasing in frequency with associated grunting.  At the end, the last ictal jerk is seen on the left.  No testing was performed during this seizure, either. 
EEG description:  At the onset of the seizure, again there is a diffuse slowing with a sharp wave seen, appears to be slightly maximal at T6, followed by polyspikes that appear to be more posterior than anterior and maximal maybe under the right posterotemporal region at T6 which appeared to evolve and settle mainly over T4, followed by a frontal spread and then generalized spreading and obscuration by muscle artifact.  Again, at the end of the seizure, there is diffuse slowing seen.   
Day #4: 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  As previous days 
SEIZURES:  None.
IMPRESSION:  The impression is abnormal EEG due to multifocal spikes from both hemispheres, at times with right predominance but seen in both hemispheres, as well as 3 seizures with unclear onset which appear to be generalized, at times with a right frontal slight predominance.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This long-term video monitoring unit is diagnostic of what appears to be multifocal localization related epilepsy with seizures possibly arising from the frontal and temporal regions.  In addition, the events during day 1 of recording had no EEG correlate.  As such, these events are not epileptic and are diagnostic psychogenic nonepileptic seizures.

LENGTH OF THE RECORDING:  58 minutes.CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 55-year-old woman found in asystole on 06/18/2013 upon arrival to the hospital.  The patient was down for at least 11 minutes.  Now she remains in a coma and has myoclonic jerks.  Past medical history notable for HIV, diabetes, hypertension, emphysema, hepatitis B, heart disease and drug abuse.MEDICATIONS:  Current medications include vecuronium 5 mg and Versed 2 mg were given during the study.  Precedex was stopped at 7 a.m. today on 06/19/2013.  Prior to that the patient was receiving propofol.CONDITION OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with an additional T1 and T2 electrode and a single EKG lead.  Additional chin EMG lead was applied.  The recording was performed at the bedside.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  At the beginning of the recording diffuse rhythmic and semi-rhythmic muscle artifact is noted.  This artifact resolves once vecuronium is administered.  Once EMG artifact resolves, the background consists of a mixture of theta and alpha activity with brief periods of discontinuity and intermittent diffuse frontal predominant sharp waves with after going slow waves.  As the recording progresses frequency of these frontal and predominant sharp waves increases gradually.  At approximately 12:44 p.m. these discharges become more rhythmic.  There is a gradual evolution of this activity into sharply contoured 3 Hz delta activity observed over the bilateral parasagittal leads.  This activity persists without significant change for the next several minutes; however, there is gradual increase in frontal and predominant rhythmic EMG artifact and it accompanies this activity.  Clinically, frontal eyebrow twitching is noted.  This activity persists until approximately 1:02 p.m. when 2 mg of Versed is administered.  Then there is a rapid resolution of EMG and rhythmic activity and evolution of the background lower to moderate amplitude relatively monomorphic alpha activity.  As the recording progresses, there is increase in amplitude and additional theta activity is observed.  Again, bifrontal sharp waves become more prominent.  These discharges again become more rhythmic at approximately 1:25 p.m. with gradual evolution into rhythmic, approximately 3 Hz sharply contoured delta activity most pronounced over the bilateral parasagittal leads.  Since the persistent sinus tachycardia with heart rate up to 150 beats per minute is noted in a single EKG lead.FINDINGS:  1. Two prolonged, greater than 15 minutes, electrographic seizures with poor localization, most pronounced over bilateral parasagittal leads.  Second seizure occurs by the end of the recording and is not fully developed.  2. Moderate diffuse slowing.3. Bifrontal poorly formed sharp waves with after going slow waves.IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 58 minute EEG due to 2 electrographic seizures, one of which is greater than 15 minutes in duration.  Additional moderate diffuse recurrent slowing is observed.  Slowing indicates etiological nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction, and in this patient is likely related to anoxic injury as well as medication affect.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology consultant team.CLINICAL HISTORY: 47 year old male with end-stage renal disease, multiple severe medical

History 
A 32-year-old male with severe underlying epilepsy, status post exacerbation of his seizures with multiple complex partial 
seizures today and a facial injury. 
INTRODUCTION: 
Digital Video EEG is performed in the lab using standard 10/20 system of electrode placement with one-channel EKG. The 
patient is awake and drowsy. 
DESCRIPTION OF RECORD: 
The background EEG is markedly abnormal. Today's EEG demonstrates excess beta, excess theta. Frequent right mid to 
posterior temporal sharp waves are observed. Although the patient is awake without clinical symptoms, he has nearly a 
pattern of electrographic seizures just prior to hyperventilation with repetitive rhythmic theta. Some of the sharp waves 
transition from mid to anterior temporal region to posterior temporal. Hyperventilation otherwise produces background 
slowing
The activity from both hemispheres is abnormal, but the activity from the right hemisphere is more significantly abnormal in 
comparison to the left. 
Drowsiness is characterized by an increase in beta and there seem to be spindles. 
During photic stimulation, he has relatively frequent right mid to posterior temporal sharp waves Photic stimulation does not 
seem to elicit seizures. 
Heart rate 100 BPM. 
IMPRESSION: 
Abnormal EEG due to: 
Prominent electrographic pattern of sharp waves and spikes in the right hemisphere, which may include right anterior 
temporal sharp waves, repetitive rhythmic activity right occipital temporal and paroxysmal rhythmic theta. 
2. Background slowing. 
3. Superimposed focal slowing on the right. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: 
This pattern was identified prior to his more recent medication changes. This is one of many EEGs for this individual and the 
pattern described today is one of many seizure patterns that he has had in the past.

Symptoms typically worsens when she stops taking her seizure medications. MRI shows right MTS. 
MEDICATIONS: Dilantin, Ativan 
INTRODUCTION: Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel of EKG. This is an awake and drowsy record. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: In wakefulness, there is an interhemispheric asymmetry with a 9 Hz alpha rhythm sometimes disrupted on the right. In addition, there is some focal slowing from the right hemisphere. In wakefulness there are high amplitude right anterior temporal spike and slow wave complexes and bursts and trains of right anterior temporal sharp waves. Testing is performed including a request for the patient to remember a word during one of these prolonged discharges, which is compatible with an electrographic seizure. The patient is able to remember a word during the discharge. 
HR: 72 bpm 
IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due to: 
1. Right temporal focal slowing. 
2. Right temporal spike and slow wave complexes. 
3. Bursts of right temporal sharp waves, which at least on 2 occasions are clearly compatible with a focal epileptiform seizure, but without clear clinical correlate. 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: The patient is more interactive with more eyes opening when she is not having the discharges. Alternative ways of evaluating the patient may include asking the patient to look at visual information rather than verbal memory which remained intact during these events. 


REASON FOR STUDY:  Differential and classification of seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY: This is a 48-year-old man with a past history of drug abuse and head trauma in the 1980s who presents with refractory epilepsy here for differential and classification. 
MEDICATIONS:  During this 24 hours the patient's Keppra was held off.  A single dose of 100 mg of Topamax was given and he was on Depakote.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed for 24 hours using 10-20 electrode placement system with the addition of anterior temporal and EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record reveals a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 9 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is normal frontocentral beta.  The patient is recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves were seen frequently in sleep and occasionally in wakefulness at F8/T2 much more frequently than F7/T3.
Focal slowing was seen intermittently and at times very rhythmically at F8/T2 at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-50 microvolts.  At times there was also seen at F7/T3.
SEIZURES:  The first complex partial seizure occurred at 11:35 a.m.  Electrographically there appeared to be an onset with rhythmic sharp waves noted at T2 in the theta frequency that increased in amplitude and became more rhythmic as well as increasing frequency spreading throughout the right hemisphere. In about 7 seconds spread to the left temporal lobe followed by the hemisphere then slowly becoming slower in frequency into the delta range and finally being obscured by muscle artifact.  After the event there appears to be diffuse slowing throughout the recording.   
Clinically, the patient remains asleep during the entirety of the electrographic seizure but once the electrographic seizure ends, he gets up and starts fumbling with the sheets and stands up and starts fumbling with the cables.  At one point he presses the alarm and when the nurse comes he does not realize that he had pressed the button.  It is not clear if he was aware of any of the events and he was not formally tested at that moment. 
Complex partial seizure number 2 occurred at 12:13:32 a.m.  Again, occurs while the patient is asleep.  The EEG reveals a rhythmic T2 sharp wave discharge that increases in amplitude and in frequency to the theta range and becomes more rhythmic as it spreads through the right hemisphere.  About 8 seconds later it starts to spread to the left anterior temporal region out of F7/T3 and then continues to spread to the rest of the hemisphere and seconds later it is obscured by muscle artifact.  The entire seizure lasts 54 seconds.  After the electrographic seizure there is diffuse slowing seen throughout the entire EEG. 
Clinically the patient appears to be asleep throughout the electrographic seizures, however, about 6 seconds after the electrographic seizure ends, he wakes, gets up from the bed, and sits up on all 4 extremities, fumbles, and looks around, and jerks back and forth and at times side to side.  About 4 or 5 
minutes later he gets out of bed and starts pulling on the cables.  Finally he removes all the cables from his head.  The RN notices this about 10 minutes later and comes into the room where he appears to not remember what happened and thinks that he was just going to the bathroom.  He was not clinically tested either at this point.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to 2 right anterior temporal seizures in addition to bilateral anterior temporal sharp waves seen slightly more prominent on the right anterior temporal region than the left mainly in sleep.
HEART RATE:  84.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with seizures possibly arising from either anterior temporal lobe.  This EEG captured 2 seizures that appeared to arise from the right anterior temporal region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the bilateral anterior temporal regions which is nonspecific with regards to the etiology.

MEDICATIONS:  Ativan, Fentanyl, Valproate Acid, Vecuronium.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Status post cardiac arrest, now with myoclonus.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during unresponsiveness and comatose.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Some artifact.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely attenuated, or almost completely suppressed background, with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  No activating procedures were performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:
During the recording there is intermittent bursts of anterior predominant spike and wave, as well as polyspike and wave appearing at 5 Hz in bursts that may last up to 20-30 seconds.  They appear to be mainly symmetric, but with some frontal predominance at times.  During these bursts the patient is seen on video to be having regular diffuse marked jerking of the truncal muscles associated with each one of these bursts.  This is most consistent with myoclonus.
Generalized attenuation of background. 
SEIZURES:  See myoclonus described above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
myoclonus and diffuse suppression of the EEG.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of diffuse cortical dysfunction and irritability, which is consistent with the known hypoxic injury.  However, it is possible that the intermixed diffuse attenuation may be at least in part due to medication effect.  Lastly, the myoclonus seen after a hypoxic injury suggests a poor prognosis for significant neurological recovery.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  67 year old gentleman with a history of seizures who presented to the hospital with recurrent seizures.  He currently remains with impaired mental status, concerning for a nonconvulsive status epilepticus.MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Versa drip, Aspirin, Vancomycin, Zosyn.INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  During maximal wakefulness, no clear posterior dominant rhythm or anterior to posterior frequency of the gradient is observed.  Instead, the recording consists of low amplitude fast activity admixed with underlying theta and occasional delta.  During periods of quiescence, increase in the amount of beta activity is present with bursts of brief, about 1 second, spindle-like activity.  No clear sleep architecture is identified.  At the end of the recording, with stimulation, frontally predominant rhythmic 6 to 7 Hz activity is captured.  It does not appear to evolve spatially or temporally, and there does not appear to be a correlation with a change in the exam.  This event lasts about 30 seconds and could potentially represent an electrographic seizure.  Tachycardia is captured on a single EKG lead.FINDINGS:  1. Excess beta.2. Moderate diffuse slowing.3. Possible primarily bifrontal 30-second electrographic seizure without clear clinical correlate during the period of stimulation.IMPRESSION/CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This is a very abnormal, about 20.02 minute EEG due to disorganized background and possible primarily bifrontal 30-second electrographic focal seizure.  These findings indicate diffuse cerebral dysfunction that in this case is partially related to medication effect, primarily from Versa drip.  A potential electrographic seizure could be a sign of an underlying focal pathology in the bifrontal regions.  The patient will be connected to long-term EEG monitoring.  These findings were discussed with the primary team.CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 54-year-old gentleman with history of lung cancer and brain metastasis, who presents with altered mental status and several episodes of seizures with focality.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote, Keppra, Versed.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed in ICU setting using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  In the comatose state, initially there were right periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges with a frequency of 1-2 Hz followed be frequent electrographic seizures characterized by 5-6 Hz sharply contoured theta activity with highest voltage in right parietal and temporal region intermixed with spike and slow wave discharges with evolution into 3 Hz sharply contoured delta activity.  They were spreading to the left cerebral hemispheres.  The above mentioned seizures activity was intermixed with right PLEDs during this recording.  This pattern continued every 3-10 seconds, lasting for up to 40 seconds.  The frequency of electrographic seizure were diminished after administration of Versed.  The background activity was low and attenuated in theta and delta range.  No clinical changes are reported with these electrographic seizures.  Heart rate was 60-90 beats per minute.
IMPRESSION:  This is an abnormal EEG recording capturing the patient in a comatose state due to:
Partial nonconvulsive status epilepticus with seizures originating from the right temporoparietal region.
Right periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges.
Diffuse slowing of background activity.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  Frequent seizures are due to the increased hyperexcitability of the underlying brain in the right cerebral hemispheres and are consistent with partial nonconvulsive status epilepticus.  Diffuse slowing of the background activity could be seen in severe toxic, metabolic, anoxic, or infectious encephalopathy.  Alternatively, it could be due to bilateral cerebral dysfunction or the effect of medications. Right hemispheric PLEDs are suggestive of underlying epileptogenicity and could be due to underlying structural lesion such as due tumor in this case.  Clinical correlation and LTM are recommended.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  65 year old woman found down after acute onset of headache in the frontal half subarachnoid status post partial coiling of an ACOM aneurysm, postprocedure noted to have left face, arm, and leg twitching. Past medical history is significant for alcohol abuse, hypertension, and diabetes.
MEDICATIONS:  Nimodipine, Gentamicin, Dilantin, Keppra.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded while comatose.  No activating procedures were performed, other than stimulation.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no clear posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  When the patient is stimulated during the recording, there is no clear change in the wave forms.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen at F4, continuously at a frequency of 0.5 to 1 Hz, representing periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLEDs).  It is apparent that with each of these discharges, although not always consistently, there seems to be clinical correlates in the middle of left shoulder twitching.
Generalized slow waves at 2 to 4 Hz with an amplitude of 20 to 70 microvolts.   
SEIZURES:  Based on the description above, it is likely that the accompaniment of the PLEDs at F4 with the left shoulder twitching is consistent with focal motor seizures.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Right frontal PLEDs and focal motor seizures. 
Generalized slow waves with intermittent periods of attenuation.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals focal motor seizures arising from the right frontal region.  In addition, there is evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regard to etiology but may be partly due to medication effect or cortical injury from the subarachnoid hemorrhage.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  54 year old man with a history of epilepsy, now with prolonged generalized tonic/clonic seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Depakote, Keppra, Phenobarbital.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Status Epilepticus
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous digital video long-term EEG was performed at bedside using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness and sleep.  No activating procedures were performed.  Continuous spike detection software as well as alarm and nurses' notes were used to review the EEG.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with a frequency of 2 to 4 Hz and an amplitude of 20 to 40 microvolts.  There is frontocentral beta seen.  No abnormal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures are performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves/PLEDS at FP1 at 1 to 2 Hz with an amplitude of 20 to 70 microvolts seen almost continuously throughout the recording, although by the last 24 hours the frequency had diminished and they were only seen in some spurts with inter bursts of more than 10 seconds.  The morphology at the end of the recording was a lot more blunt and of lower amplitude.
Generalized slow waves. 
SEIZURES:  During the recording, the patient would have, at the FP1 electrode, rhythmic discharges that would increase in frequency and decrease in amplitude, and then suddenly stop multiple times per hour for the first 10 hours.  Suddenly they subsided and stopped, and for the rest of the recording none were seen.  During the ones that occurred at the beginning of the recording, no clear clinical correlation was noted.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:
Seizures from the left prefrontal region which subsided after the first 10 hours, and none were seen for the last 50 hours of recording.
Focal sharp waves/PLEDS seen in the left prefrontal region.
Generalized slow waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of acute structural dysfunction and irritability arising from the left prefrontal region which has a high correlation with an acute structural lesion in that region.  In addition, there is evidence of seizures arising from the same region for the first 10 hours of the recording and none seen in the next 50 hours.  There is also evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regard to etiology and is likely due, at least in part, to medication effect.  These changes were noted to the primary team during the recording.

CLINICAL HISTORY: 87 year old man with history of DVT, PE, hypertension, esophageal ulcer, angioplasty, that was found down confused by his son, now with a right frontal mass.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Haldol, Lorazepam, Hydralazine, Dexamethasone, Insulin.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Confusion and change in mental status.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness, but was lethargic.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Some movement artifact during the recording.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to diffusely slow recording with a posterior dominant rhythm that at best reaches 7 Hz and is reactive to eye opening.  There is lack of normal frontal central beta seen.  The patient is only recorded in wakefulness and no activating procedures were performed. 
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:
Focal sharp waves seen at C4/F4 at times in rhythmic bursts of 2 Hz consistent with periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges (PLED'S) almost continuously throughout the recording.
Focal slow waves seen at C4/F4 at 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-100 mV also seen continuously throughout the recording.
Generalized slow waves at 5-7 Hz with an amplitude of 20-60 mV seen continuously throughout the recording.  
SEIZURES:  During the recording at one point the rhythmic PLED'S appeared to increase in frequency and evolve to infrequency and decreasing amplitude for about 1 minute and 20 seconds.  In the video there does not appear to be a clinical correlate which is consistent with an electrographic seizure.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Right frontal central PLED'S. 
One electrographic seizure. 
Generalized slow waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals one single electrographic seizure coming from the right fronto-central region. In addition, there is evidence of acute structural dysfunction and irritability in the right frontal central region which is consistent with the known frontal mass.  There was also evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific in regards to etiology and may be due, at least in part, to medication effect.


LENGTH OF THE RECORDING:  Twenty-five minutes.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 42-year-old man who seizures since childhood, who now presents with increase in seizure frequency.
MEDICATIONS:  Tegretol, Depakote, and Ativan given at an outside emergency room.
CONDITIONS OF THE RECORDING:  The recording was performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single lead EKG lead.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background consists of polymorphic low to moderate amplitude diffuse theta admixed with occasional delta.  During sleep, the degree of delta increases.  Brief bifrontal fast activity is captured.  A single electrographic seizure is captured.  Clinically, it occurs out of sleep when the technologist wakes up the patient for hyperventilation.  During the seizure, the patient does not respond and appears to have possible right hand systemic posturing.  Electrographically, there is rapid diffuse voltage attenuation and appearance of diffuse fast activity that is most pronounced bifrontally.  The seizure lasts approximately 35-40 seconds.  Hyperventilation is performed with poor effort and does not result in significant change in the background.  Photic stimulation does not result in additional abnormalities.
FINDINGS:  
Mild to moderate diffuse slowing.  
Occasional bifrontal brief fast activity.  
A single clinical and electrographic seizure out of wakefulness of unclear lateralization, possibly bifrontal in onset.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 25-minute awake and asleep EEG due to mild to moderate diffuse slowing, bifrontal fast activity that is likely to be epileptiform in nature, and a single approximately 40 second seizure out of sleep of unclear lateralization, possibly frontal in onset.  Diffuse slowing indicates nonspecific cerebral dysfunction.   Epileptiform discharges and seizure indicate near cortical origin of the patient's epilepsy.  These findings were conveyed to the neurology primary team at the time of the review.


REASON FOR EEG:  Refractory seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 42-year-old man with a history of epilepsy since he was 5 years old, who presents noncompliant with medications and with recurrent refractory seizures.  EEG to quantify and differentiate seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Tegretol and Depakote, as well as Keppra.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  No activation procedures were performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  At about 11 p.m., the patient started becoming agitated and moving and disconnected multiple of his leads and by 1 in the morning, unfortunately the majority of his leads were lost and the recording could not be continued.  This is only a 17-hour recording.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches at best 6-7 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is some frontal central beta.  The patient does appear to go into sleep, but the sleep architecture is not typical.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
There are rare bifrontal sharp waves seen in sleep, which appear to be maximal at possibly F4 with a prefrontal field.  
Generalized slowing at 5-7 Hz seen continuously throughout the recording.
SEIZURES:  Three seizures were recorded during this long-term monitoring.  The first one occurred at 10:15 in the morning.  Clinically, the patient is laying in bed and appears to grunt and suddenly jerks.  It lasts for what appears to be 20 seconds.  Electrographically at the beginning of the seizure, there is predominantly frontal beta discharge with associated attenuation that appears in both frontal regions and appears to continue as it is obscured by muscle artifact.  Afterwards, there appears to be some slowing in the frontal region.  Although the slowing is difficult to distinguish from its usual background.  The second seizure occurred at 2:41 p.m., lasting for 30 seconds.  This one occurs from sleep.  The patient suddenly moves his right arm and the right hand clearly becomes dystonic suddenly, the right arm then becomes forcefully extended, he grunts, and suddenly the seizure stops.  The nurse came into the room, but did not test him until the seizure had clinically and electrographically stopped.  Electrographically at onset there appears to be again a frontal predominant beta discharge and diffuse attenuation, which continues to evolve and then gets obscured by muscle artifact.  After the event, there appears to be some more prominent diffuse slowing.  Seizure number 3 occurs at 8:51 p.m., about 40 seconds after waking up.  The patient suddenly grunts and his right arm moves underneath the covers and cannot be seen.  This lasts about 30 seconds and then suddenly stops.  Electrographically again there is a frontal beta discharge, which is associated with diffuse attenuation and appears to continue to evolve and then no longer seen due to muscle artifact.  After the seizure appears to clinically end, there seems to be some diffuse slowing.   
Heart rate is 86.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to 3 seizures likely arising from either frontal lobe in addition to diffuse slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with 3 seizures likely arising from the frontal region with unclear lateralization.  The right arm dystonic posture in one of them suggests a possible left frontal onset, although this cannot be certain.  These seizures were communicated to the team as the EEG was performed.  Lastly, there is evidence of moderate diffuse cerebral dysfunction, which is nonspecific with regards to etiology.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 46-year-old man with history of recurrent intracranial hemorrhage, episodes of unclear etiology who now presents with new left temporal hemorrhage and multiple seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Current medications Keppra, loaded with Dilantin, phenobarbital, and placed on Versed drip later in the recording.
CONDITION OF THE RECORDING:  The recording is performed according to the standard 10-20 system with additional T1-T2 electrodes and a single EKG lead.  CT compatible electrodes were used.  The recording was reviewed intermittently throughout the 24 hour period by an  attending neurologist.  Medical staff notes and nursing staff notes were reviewed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is right-to-left interhemispheric asymmetry with lower waveforms and high amplitudes over the left hemisphere.  The background over the right hemisphere has a posterior dominant rhythm of approximately 8-9 Hz activity with weak anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  There is additional polymorphic theta activity observed over the right hemisphere.  The posterior dominant rhythm over the left hemisphere consists of approximately 8 Hz theta activity without clear anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background over the left hemisphere consists of a mixture of polymorphic theta and delta activity.  Throughout the recording additional diffuse low amplitude rhythmic slow delta activity is captured.  As the recording progresses, frequency of this delta activity becomes more pronounced.  Additionally as the recording progresses the patient appears to start having poorly formed electrographic seizures emanating from the left temporal parietal region.  These seizures are characterized by rhythmic theta activity most pronounced over the left lateral leads.  As the recording progresses seizures appear to spread diffusely and are characterized by diffuse rhythmic delta activity, with associated blink artifact.  Clinically the patient appears to have a rhythmic bilateral eye blinking.  Ativan was administered but did not result in cessation of status epilepticus.  The patient was subsequently loaded with IV Dilantin.  This resulted in resolution of generalized status epilepticus; however, he continued to have focal left temporal parietal status epilepticus.  The patient was subsequently intubated at 11:52 p.m. using propofol.  This resulted in abrupt discontinuation of status epilepticus as well as diffuse burst suppression.  That lasted several minutes.  As burst suppression decreased discrete moderate amplitude sharp waves were observed over the left temporoparietal region.  Their frequency gradually increased to approximately 1 Hz.  These discharges constituted PLEDs.  These discharges are accompanied by lower amplitude fast activity thus representing PLEDs-plus.  Occasionally frequency of these discharges increases but never lasts longer than 10 seconds.  This constitutes a brief ictal rhythmic discharges (BIRDS).
FINDINGS:  Left temporal parietal seizures that slowly evolved into generalized status epilepticus.  PLEDs and PLEDs-plus emanating from the left temporal parietal region observed after 12 a.m..  Diffuse background slowing and discontinuation.  Focal left hemispheric slowing.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal 24-hour continuous audio video electroencephalogram  monitoring due to gradual resolution of focal left temporal parietal status epilepticus that evolves into generalized status epilepticus.  __________ status epilepticus resolves with administration of Ativan and Dilantin.  However, focal left temporal parietal status epilepticus persists.  It resolves with administration of anesthetic  agent and initiation of phenobarbital.  However, periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges are still observed at that time.  Diffuse background slowing indicates __________ nonspecific diffuse cerebral dysfunction.  Focal left hemispheric slowing indicates focal cerebral dysfunction, likely structural etiology and in this patient is partially related to postictal phenomenon.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology primary team.

The patient removed his EEG electrodes within minutes of the first electrode placement and electrodes were replaced at the bedside and the patient was placed in restraints.DESCRIPTION OF RECORD:  As the recording begins, the patient is awake with a pattern with generous frontal central beta, diffuse theta, and a suggestion of a 9 Hz alpha rhythm.  There are intermittent bursts of sharply contoured theta from the left temporal region. While the patient is awake and alert and not experiencing a seizure, he begins to remove his EEG electrodes at 7:17 p.m. The patient has an electrographic seizure at 7:20:08 lasting  until 7:20:36.  This is characterized by rhythmic theta from the left. Despite the problems with the electrodes, there is additional visible recording from the left temporal region through 8 o'clock and no other electrographic seizures were identified.  As the recording continues, the value of the EEG is declining because of the lack of electrodes. No additional clinical seizures were noted. Heart rate 66 BPM.IMPRESSION:  This video EEG monitoring session was remarkable for only really 1 hour of good quality data, but a brief electrographic seizure from the left anterior temporal region.
