CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 40-year-old woman evaluated for new onset seizures in March, the patient admitted with tonic-clonic seizure and then was increasingly confused.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote, Keppra, oxycodone, Ativan, others.
INTRODUCTION:  Continuous EEG monitoring was performed in the unit using standard 10-20 system of electrode placement with 1 channel EKG.  The patient had received Ativan in the EEG lab because of electrographic seizures associated with confusion.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  In the initial hours of the EEG, the patient demonstrated an ongoing, ictal/postictal pattern.  As noted in the baseline EEG, the patient appeared to have a later form of status epilepticus at the beginning with periodic complexes and rhythmic epileptiform activity.  Ongoing ictal activity with clear cut, rhythmic, repetitive theta activity was noted as early as 12:18.  The activity is best reviewed in an average reference with a high amplitude, anterior temporal sharp waves, and fast activity.  Repetitive seizures are noted with waxing and waning in the right anterior temporal region maximum T2/S8.  Additional more clearly defined seizures are seen at 2:58, 3:27. 
The patient is quite restless. She has unusual behavior, but is often communicative.  In some cases, the complex partial seizures are not recognized by the family.  The patient has unusual behavior, turning to the left, ignoring the family, but the family does not seem to recognize this as epileptiform activity.  Additional ictal discharges were noted at 6:06 and 6:11.  Overall, in the initial hours, the activity seems to wax and wane from an ictal to a postictal pattern.  This activity seems to be abating by 9:29.  Unfortunately, the patient starts removing the epileptiform activity.  With the available data by 11 a.m. seems did demonstrate a reduction in the periodic complexes in the right frontotemporal and anterior temporal region. 
Unfortunately, due to the patient's behavior, there is no additional valuable data after 11 p.m. 
Heart rate: /NA.
IMPRESSION:  Day 1 of continuous EEG monitoring was remarkable for:
Complex partial seizures noted in the initial hours of recording, gradually slowing by 11:00 p.m.
Ongoing confusion.
Markedly slow pattern noted from the right hemisphere with focal slowing in the periodic activity.  
More of a theta pattern on the left. 
Clinical correlation:  This EEG is diagnostic of a status epilepticus pattern, but the pattern did appear to be improving by the end of this section of the record.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  The patient is a 71-year-old woman with a history of cryptococcal meningitis who remains in unexplained coma.  An EEG is performed to rule out status epilepticus.
MEDICATIONS:  Lopressor, prednisone, Lovenox, metoprolol, and Protonix. The patient is not getting Versed.
INTRODUCTION:  The recording was performed according to standard 10/20 system with additional T1 and T2 electrodes and a single-EKG lead.  Hyperventilation and photic stimulation were not performed.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  There is no posterior dominant rhythm and no anterior to posterior frequency amplitude gradient.  The background consists of approximately 3 second bursts of variable amplitude polymorphic delta activity.  Bursts of activity are generally poorly formed over the right.  Inter-burst interval last approximately 4-7 seconds on average, thus making burst suppression ratio approximately 1:1 to 1:3.  No normal sleep architecture is captured.  Independent bursts of polymorphic sharply contoured fast activity are captured over the left posterior temporal/posterior quadrant.  Four electrographic seizures are captured.  These seizures all differ electrographically.  The seizures occurred at approximately 11:48 a.m., 11:51 a.m., 12:03 p.m., and 12:38, and last approximately 1 minute 39 seconds, 1 minute 23 seconds, 1 minute 15 seconds, and approximately 3 minutes and 16 seconds, respectively.  First electrographic seizure starts with moderate amplitude sharply contoured fast activity emanating from the left posterior temporal region.  This activity rapidly evolves into approximately 2.5 Hz rhythmic delta that increases in amplitude and decreases in frequency and spreads to the bilateral occipital region.   
Seizure number 2 starts over the left lateral leads either over the left posterior temporal region or left anterior temple region.  Seizure starts with a low amplitude discrete spikes with after-going slow waves that rapidly increase in frequency into the alpha range (approximately 11 Hz activity) and spread throughout the left temporal region and bilateral occipital regions.  This activity gradually decreases in frequency and increases in amplitude, and spreads throughout the left hemisphere.  By the end of the seizure, the seizure is characterized by approximately 1 Hz 0.3 second bursts of moderate amplitude, rhythmic, alpha activity.   
Seizure number 3 is similar in appearance and evolution to seizure number 2, however, it starts over the right temporal region.   
Seizure number 4 appears to start over the right anterior temporal/mid temporal region.  It starts with a 5 Hz small amplitude sharp waves with after-going slow waves that rapidly decrease in frequency.  As the seizure progresses it appears to stop over the right temporal region and start over the left posterior temporal region.  As the seizure progresses it is similar in appearance to seizure number 1.   
There is no clear clinical correlation with any of the patient's seizures.  There is no clear reactivity to either noxious, tactile, or verbal stimuli.  An irregular heart rate is noted in the single EKG lead.
FINDINGS:  
Burst suppression, with burst suppression ratio of approximately 1:1 up to 1:3.  
Near lack of clear bursts over the right temporal region.  
Four discrete electrographic seizures that differ in appearance and emanate from either left posterior temporal region, left anterior temporal region, and right anterior temporal region. 
No clear evidence of reactivity.
IMPRESSION:  This is a very abnormal nearly 24-minute coma EEG due to  1:1 to 1:3 burst suppression, poorly formed bursts over the right temporal region, and 4 discrete electrographic seizures that differ electrographically and that arise from at least 3 distinct cerebral regions.  These findings indicate significant cerebral dysfunction  and if the patient is not sedative medications indicate a poor prognosis of a full neurological recovery.  These findings were conveyed to the Neurology consultant team at that time of the review.  The patient will be converted to the long term monitoring.


The patient was recorded while unresponsive.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  A long-term EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, metoprolol, Protonix, Lovenox, prednisone, Lopressor, midazolam. The patient was also given Versed for a short period of time.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record reveals a background that is completely attenuated and suppressed with intermixed seizures and sharp waves which I will describe in abnormal discharges.  There is no normal sleep architecture.  There is no normal frontocentral beta.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen intermittently at T5, at times they appear to be polyspike discharges at the same electrode.  There are also sharp waves seen at T3 and F7 as well as O1 and T6 although these appear independently.  
The background appears to be completely suppressed and attenuated in between the sharp waves and the seizures.
SEIZURES:  
There are multiple electrographic seizures occurring almost continuously throughout the recording with no clinical correlate.  Some appear to occur at T5, O1, at times at T3, sometimes at T4 and T6 with polyspikes that appear to evolve and spread sometimes from the right hemisphere to the left hemisphere, sometimes remain on the left occipital or right mid temporal region.  These seizures may last for 2-3 minutes and start and stop and at times there are short rhythmic runs bursts of sharp waves seen.  As described before in between the seizures there is complete attenuation of the EEG and at times intermixed with delta discharges that are sharply contoured in bursts.  Some seizures appear to terminate in the left frontal region at F7 but again sometimes they switch from the right hemisphere to the left hemisphere as they evolve.  By about 8 o'clock, the background appears to attenuate and the seizure appeared to improve in amplitude and some T4 seizures are seen without lower amplitude and less polymorphic activity.  They continue to occur and appear to remain as T4 PLEDS for some time until about 8:15 p.m. after which no more electrical activity can be seen except some artifact.  At around 8:30 p.m. or slightly before, the background appears to become completely suppressed and attenuated with no clear electrocerebral activity seen and this remains for the rest of the recording.  Of note, around this time the patient was given some Versed.  The EEG then remains flat for the entirety of the recording until the next day.  During the morning the next day around 10:30, the patient was placed on an electrocerebral silence protocol and reviewed under 2 microvolts of sensitivity, there is no clear electrocerebral activity seen.  
Heart rate: 72.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to nonconvulsive electrical status epilepticus arising from multiple foci with additional intermixed diffuse attenuation which progresses to complete electrocerebral silence throughout the recording.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  In this EEG the first hours reveal nonconvulsive electrographic status epilepticus arising from multiple foci as described above. In addition, there is evidence of marked diffuse cerebral dysfunction seen between the seizures which appears to progress at the end of the record to overall to electrocerebral silence.  Of note, medication was given during the process of change which may at least in part affect the findings seen on the EEG. The severe suppression was noted and informed to the primary team.


REASON FOR STUDY:  Brain tumor and seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  A 69-year-old man with a history of brain tumor in the right frontal region status post radiation, epilepsy, hypertension and diabetes with increased seizures after surgery, now with increased seizures about twice per month, described as tonic-clonic with no loss of consciousness for 4 to 5 minutes afterwards or a bad in the mouth.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Keppra, metoprolol, metformin.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with the addition of anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and drowsiness.  Activating procedures included hyperventilation.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Movement throughout the recording.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record reveals a slow background with a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 9-10 Hz.  The patient is recorded mainly in wakefulness.  Activating procedures included hyperventilation that was performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Focal slow waves seen continuously over the right hemisphere, maximal in the central parietal and temporal regions at a frequency of 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.  There is also increased beta over that region consistent with a breached rhythm.
SEIZURES:  During the recording there were about 4 seizures which appeared to begin over the right hemisphere at T4/C4 and increasing amplitude and frequency from delta to theta, and seconds later appeared to spread to the left hemisphere in the T5 electrode which appeared to continue to evolve and then suddenly stop.  During the event the patient is tested and at the time is slow to respond, but not clearly unresponsive.  The seizures last about 1 minute and 45 seconds, otherwise there is no other EEG correlate.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to right central temporal and parietal continuous focal slowing and breach in addition to multiple seizures likely arising from that region
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with seizures arising around the right central parietal region.  Multiple of what appeared to be possible electrographic versus complex partial seizures.  In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the right central parietal and temporal region which is consistent with the known tumor and radiation.  These findings were informed to the patient.  He was sent to the emergency room for admission and further treatment of his seizures.

CLINICAL HISTORY:  48 year old with history of seizures status post code, now with seizures versus myoclonus.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Trileptal, Keppra, Versed, Ativan.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Twitching, seizures
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video long-term EEG was performed for 53 hours at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system and additional anterior temporal single-lead EKG electrode and bilateral arm EMG electrodes.  The patient was unresponsive during the exam.  The EEG was reviewed using continuous spike detection and seizure detection software along with nurses' notes and patient alarms.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no clear posterior dominant rhythm or frontocentral beta.  There are diffuse periods of attenuation as well as intermittent bursts of anterior maximal 8 Hz discharge that come intermittently throughout the recording.  In addition, there is asymmetric anterior predominant small amplitude polyspikes and generalized spike and waves that at times have an EMG correlate noted in the arms and in the video on the face.  It is sometimes difficult to assess if there is always a precedent generalized spike and wave or polyspike prior to the muscle artifact.  However, there are definitely times where there is muscle artifact and there is no clear spike seen on the recording.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Polyspikes and generalized spike and waves seen asymmetrically, at times symmetrically during the recording with associated muscle artifact. 
Generalized slow waves and intermittent bursts of suppression.
SEIZURES:  As above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Markedly diffuse slow waves. 
Myoclonic jerks.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of diffuse cerebral dysfunction and cortical damage, which is consistent with a known hypoxic injury.  However, some of this slowing may be due at least in part to medication effect.  The episodes of jerks associated with anterior predominant polyspike and waves are consistent with the clinical diagnosis of myoclonus.  The overall background and presence of myoclonus suggests a poor prognosis.

REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was unresponsive during the recording.  No activation procedures were performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background which at best has a frequency of 2-4 Hz with an amplitude of 20-40 mV.  There is no normal posterior dominant rhythm or frontal central beta seen.  No normal sleep architecture is seen.  No activating procedures were performed.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES: 
There are generalized polyspike and spike and waves in bursts with anterior predominance seen symmetrically over both hemispheres at a frequency of 0.5-2 Hz, which have most of the correlate seen in the video.  
In between these bursts of generalized anterior predominant spike and waves, there is intermittent bursts of diffuse suppression that may last 2-3 seconds.  
Some of these generalized spike and waves and polyspikes at times become rhythmic for about 1.5 seconds, which produce a tonic response seen on video. 
SEIZURES:  Myoclonic and tonic events as described above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Myoclonus, as well as a severely low amplitude. 
Generalized slow background.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with severe diffuse cerebral dysfunction and cortical damage which is consistent with a hypoxic injury.  The multiple myoclonic seizures seen during the recording are suggestive of a poor prognosis.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  66 year old man with hypertension, diabetes, CRD, motor vehicle accident with subsequent intracranial hemorrhage, felt shortness of breath, collapsed at home, was taken to the emergency room, 10 minutes of arrest.  Prior EEG on 01/27/2012, now in continuous myoclonus.
MEDICATIONS:  Depakote.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Myoclonus.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed at the bedside using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrodes.  The patient was recorded with no stimulation, and he was comatose during the exam.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely slow background with no clear posterior dominant rhythm, frontocentral beta, or variability.  There is no activating procedures.  During the recording there are diffuse bursts of attenuation intermixed with infrequent bursts of, at times, generalized polyspike seen maximal anterior and spike and wave that sometimes can be seen prior to EMG muscle correlate and sometimes are not seen before EMG muscle correlate, which on video is consistent with myoclonus and twitching of the lower extremities.  During the exam, the patient is stimulated with noxious stimuli, variable, and sound stimuli, and there is no improvement of the EEG frequencies.   
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Polyspike discharges with associated muscle activity consistent with myoclonus.
Generalized slowing at 2 to 4 Hz with microvolts of 20 to 40 microvolts amplitude with intermittent burst suppression and attenuation in the background. 
SEIZURES:  See above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to:
Myoclonus.
Severely slow and attenuated background.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of diffuse cortical dysfunction and irritability consistent with myoclonus.  These findings are suggestive of poor prognosis, especially in the setting of hypoxic brain injury.


CLINICAL HISTORY:  57 year old man status post PEA code for 15 minutes, found down for some unknown period of time.
MEDICATIONS:  Heparin, Zosyn, Dopamine, Norepinephrine, Midazolam, Fentanyl, Versed.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Status post PEA code, found down.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while unresponsive.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a diffusely suppressed background with no normal posterior dominant rhythm or normal frontocentral beta.  Intermixed with this diffuse attenuation, there are bursts of at times frontally predominant spike discharge followed by muscle artifact.  At times this frontally predominant spike is not seen but there is slowing and muscle artifact.  Clinically, the patient appears to open his eyes during these burst that last as long as 2 seconds and appears to tremble slightly.  The patient is suctioned during the study and there is no change in the pattern in the EEG.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Diffuse suppression.
Bursts of anterior predominant spike and slow wave discharge followed by slow waves and muscle artifact along with associated clinical response of eye opening and trembling consistent with myoclonus.
SEIZURES:  See above.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Generalized suppression and generalized spike and wave discharges consistent with myoclonus.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of marked diffuse cerebral dysfunction and irritability, which is consistent with the known hypoxic injury.  The marked suppression pattern along with the myoclonus is suggestive of a poor prognosis.


CLINICAL HISTORY: 63 year old woman with an episode a month ago of loss of consciousness with confusion, has occurred 2 times per week, has significant hypertension, COPD, aneurysm, asthma, infarction, and history of seizures.
MEDICATIONS:  Neurontin, Lopressor, Norvasc, Remeron, also supposed to be taking Keppra, unfortunately in the last note from the doctor, appears that she was noncompliant.
REASON FOR STUDY:  History of seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded while awake and drowsy.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well-defined posterior dominant rhythm that reaches at 10-11 Hz seen mainly over the left hemisphere and somewhat attenuated and interrupted over the left hemisphere.  There is a normal amount of frontocentral beta.  No activating procedures were performed.  The patient was recorded only in wakefulness and drowsiness.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  
Focal sharp waves seen intermittently at T6, sometimes at C4/P4.  
Focal slow waves seen continuously over the right hemisphere, maximum over the anterior temporal region at F8/T4, but sometimes more at C4/F4.
Rhythmic run of C4 sharp wave with some evolution.
SEIZURES:  There were 4 seizures that began at electrode T6 with evolving low amplitude beta frequency that decreases in frequency, but increases in amplitude and evolves to sharp waves and spreads to the right occipital region and right parietal quadrant, then stops suddenly.  The seizures last anywhere from 1 minute to 2 minutes.  During the events, there appears to be no clinical correlate and during one of them, the technician asks the patient to count backwards while it is occurring and the patient is able to perform the command with no difficulty.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
4 electrographic right posterotemporal seizures.  
Right hemispheric slowing maximally in the anterior temporal region.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization-related epilepsy with seizures arising from the right posterotemporal and central region.  In addition, there is evidence of focal structural cerebral dysfunction in the right hemisphere, maximal in the anterior temporal region, which is consistent with the known stroke.  The findings in the EEG were communicated to her primary care doctor and multiple calls were made to the patient to make her aware of her diagnosis.  However, no answer was obtained.


CLINICAL HISTORY: 57 year old man with a history of hypertension and epilepsy, the last one occurring yesterday.
MEDICATIONS:  Keppra, Hydrochlorothiazide.
REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
INTRODUCTION:  Digital video routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with anterior temporal and since lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness and drowsiness.  Activating procedures included hyperventilation and photic stimulation.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a well-defined posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 8.5 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is no normal frontal central beta.  Activating procedures including hyperventilation and photic stimulation produce no abnormal discharges.  The patient was recorded in wakefulness and drowsiness.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Focal slow waves seen at F7/D3 and at times T3 occasionally at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and an amplitude of 20-60 microvolts.
SEIZURES:  During the EEG recording, the patient reports feeling his normal aura of smell of ammonia.  During that time, the EEG remained unremarkable with no changes.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Left anterior temporal and mid temporal focal slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG reveals evidence of focal cerebral dysfunction in the left anterior temporal and mid temporal regions, which is nonspecific in regards to etiology.  The episode of where the patient report smelling “ammonia” that was captured, had no EEG correlate.  As such, it may represent simple partial seizure which up to 75% of the time has no EEG correlate.  No clear seizures or epileptiform discharges were seen.


REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizure.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  An 18-year-old woman with a history of seizures since age 2, seizure free until age 10, began to have seizures during sleep every night.  Reports tongue biting, bladder incontinence, and no recall of events.
MEDICATIONS:  Carbatrol.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and sleep.  Activating procedures were performed including photic stimulation and hyperventilation.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  None.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The records reveals a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 9 Hz which is reactive to eye opening.  There is normal frontal central beta.  The patient is recorded in wakefulness and sleep. 
Activating procedures included hyperventilation and photic stimulation. 
Abnormal discharges:
Frequent focal sharp waves seen at F7/T4 at times with a more posterior component at T6 and sometimes more of anterior component at FP2.  These occurred frequently during wakefulness and sleep. 
Focal sharp waves seen rarely at F7/T3 during wakefulness and sleep. 
Seizures:  One seizure occurred at 9:48:10 a.m. where the patient suddenly while asleep opened her eyes and appears to be startled.  She moves her eyes to the right and turns her head to the right and starts moaning.  Next, her right arm starts shaking back and forth and she continues to do this for some seconds until her shoulders start shaking and then she starts shaking the left arm.  Next, the head moves to the left and then she starts moving side to side while moaning.  At this point the left hand appears to be having some automatisms and she starts arching her back until the seizure ends. 
Electrographically at the onset of the seizure there appeared to be a bilateral prefrontal discharge which only causes the left hemisphere to become attenuated and muscle artifact starts forming.  At that point central alpha discharge is noticed, specifically a CZ, which then involves into a diffuse theta and delta slowing seen in both hemispheres.  As the patient's head moves side to side, the rhythmicity continues to evolve and suddenly stops.  Afterwards there is diffuse slowing. 
Heart rate:  108.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to right, much more than left, anterior temporal sharp waves and a single seizure of unclear onset, but appears to be possibly left prefrontal with quick spread centrally.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with seizures possibly arising from either anterior temporal lobe or the frontal lobe.


CLINICAL HISTORY: 73 year old right handed male admitted to Nursing Facility due to Dilantin toxicity who presented yesterday to the Outpatient Neurology Clinic with report of 3 seizures and proceeded to have complex partial seizures in the office with a return to the baseline, now admitted to the Intensive Care Unit for complex partial status epilepticus.
MEDICATIONS:  Dilantin, Tylenol.
REASON FOR EEG:  Rule out status.
INTRODUCTION:  Long-term digital video EEG was performed at bedside for 12 hours using standard 10-20 electrode placement system and additional anterior temporal single lead EKG electrode.  Seizure spike detection software was used along with nursing reports of events and review of the EEG.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  At the onset of the EEG, there was an increased amount of movement artifact.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  At the onset of record, the background appeared to be mildly slow with a posterior dominant rhythm that at best reaches 8 Hz but appears to be reactive to eye opening and closing.  There is a normal amount of frontocentral beta.  Patient during the recording does go into the first stages of sleep.  No activating procedures were performed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  In the recording, there are, specifically at the beginning, frequent focal sharp waves seen at T4/T6, at times moving more anteriorly to T2/F8.  In addition, there are infrequent O2 sharp waves and T1/F7 sharp waves are also seen.  The sharp waves at times appear to become rhythmic without evolution, specifically the ones on the T2/T6 electrode.
SEIZURES:  Multiple seizures were seen at the beginning of the recording that appeared to be arising mainly from the right temporal region, specifically at the T4/T6 electrodes.  However, sometimes the seizures appeared to start or spike quickly to the left temporal region and it is difficult or unclear to note the onset.  At times, they also appeared to ping-pong from the right to the left hemisphere in the temporal region before stopping and starting.  The patient appeared to move around in bed, sometimes rhythmically  moving the right hand during the events and staring, but if talked to, would be able to answer questions simply.  After about 10:50 p.m. the seizures subsided and only about 3 of them were seen which were all arising from the right temporal region.  The last one was noted this morning around the end of the recording.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to: 
Right more than left temporal lobe seizures. 
Right temporal sharp waves. 
Right posterior temporal occipital sharp waves.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This long-term EEG revealed evidence of partial status epilepticus arising from the temporal lobes.  The seizures, however, subsequently markedly reduced in number after two hours into the recording.  In addition, there was evidence of bitemporal focal cerebral dysfunction and diffuse cerebral dysfunction, both of which are nonspecific with regards to etiology.  These findings were communicated to the primary team.


REASON FOR STUDY: Rule out seizures. 
HISTORY: A 57-year-old woman with recent onset of staring spells for about 2 days lasting for 30 seconds. These spells have consisted o f mainly left sided gaze deviation. The patient also has a history of breast cancer and HIV. 
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES: None. 
MEDICATIONS: Methadone, Protonix, lovenox, and azithromycin. 
INTRODUCTION: A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with additional anterior temporal and single-lead EKG electrode. The patient was recorded while confused and drowsy. Activating procedures were not performed. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD: The record opens up to a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 8 5 Hz which appears to be reactive to eye-opening. There IS some frontocentral beta seen. The patient is recorded mainly In wakefulness and drowsiness. Activating procedures were not performed. 
DISCHARGES: Excessive theta in wakefulness at 5-7 Hz seen throughout the recording at times with intermixed delta. 
SEIZURES: During the recording there were 3 seizures seen. On the EEG there appears to be right frontal. Specifically at F4, theta discharge that appears to spread throughout the right central and right frontal regions and continues to increase In amplitude and decrease in frequency remaining mainly over the right hemisphere. As the seizure continues it slows down in frequency to a high amplitude delta sharp waves that are maximal at F4. The seizures last for about 1 minute. Clinically. The patient appears to lift up the head and look towards the left. It is not clear if she is unresponsive throughout the event as there is no good audio on the video for the EEG. 
IMPRESSION: Abnormal EEG due lo 3 seizures arising from the right frontal region in addition to moderate diffuse slowing.
 
CLINICAL CORRELATION: This EEG is consistent with a diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with 3 seizures arising from the right frontal region. In addition, there is evidence of mild to moderate diffuse which IS nonspecific with regards to etiology. These findings were reported to the as they were seen. 


REASON FOR STUDY:  Seizures.
CLINICAL HISTORY:  This is a 42-year-old woman with a history of seizures, hepatitis C, drug abuse on methadone, who presents with complaints of heart racing, dizzy, feeling shaky with no loss of consciousness.  She has had seizures for 9 years, last one was 1 week ago.
MEDICATIONS:  Trileptal, aspirin, Lovenox, gabapentin, zonisamide.
INTRODUCTION:  A routine EEG was performed using the standard 10-20 electrode placement system with a lead anterior temporal and a single lead EKG electrode.  The patient was recorded during wakefulness and drowsiness.  Activating procedures were not performed.
TECHNICAL DIFFICULTIES:  Some movement artifact intermittently.
DESCRIPTION OF THE RECORD:  The record opens to a posterior dominant rhythm that reaches 8 Hz which appears to be reactive to eye opening.  There is some frontocentral beta.  Activating procedures included hyperventilation produced no abnormal discharges.  Throughout the recording, the patient has intermittent bouts of nystagmoid eye movements while her eyes are closed.
ABNORMAL DISCHARGES:  Focal sharp wave seen at 01 intermittently, in addition to focal slowing seen at also 01-02 in the parietal region at a frequency of 2-4 Hz and amplitude of 20-60 mV.
SEIZURES:  The patient had a seizure at 1:19:46 p.m. which clinically the patient appears to move her head to the left and then to the right.  Then suddenly her eyes deviate towards the right and her head starts turning towards the right while her hands are clenched in front of her.  She remains in a stiff posturing with her eyes deviated to the right and her head deviated to the right throughout the entire seizure.  She intermittently swallows while this is occurring and smacks her lips.  It appears that at 1:21:20, the seizure ends electrographically, however, clinically she continues to stare and appears to continue to lip smack and mumbles.  The patient remains confused until almost the end of the recording.
EEG:  It appears the electrographic onset to be a sudden change in attenuation over the right hemisphere, losing the posterior dominant frequency.  This continues to evolve with frontal theta discharge that appears as the patient starts moving her head to the right, then spreads to the occipital region on the left hemisphere.  It continues to quickly increase and evolve amplitude and decrease in frequency until it is obscured by muscle artifact.  There is a buildup and spread of this rhythmicity and which suddenly it stops and is followed by diffuse slowing with intermixed muscle artifact on the EEG.   
Heart rate 84.
IMPRESSION:  Abnormal EEG due to a single seizure, of unclear origin, in addition to some left occipital sharp waves and slowing.
CLINICAL CORRELATION:  This EEG is consistent with the diagnosis of localization related epilepsy with a single seizure of unclear origin. In addition, there is evidence of focal cerebral structural dysfunction and irritability in the left occipital area.
